فصل في الشهادة على الإرث
ولَزِمَ الجُرُّ الشاهدِ الإرث بقوله مات وتركه ميراثاً له ، أو مات وذا في ملكه ، أو في يده ، فإن قال : كان لأبيه إعارة ، أو أودعه أو اجاره من في يده جاز بلا جر
[1]
ولَزِمَ الجر الشاهد الإرث بقولهِ مات وتركه ميراثاً له ، أو مات وذا في ملكه، أو في يده) : أي إذا قال الشُّهود كان هذا المورث هذا المدعي لا يُقْضَى للوارث حتَّى يجروا الميراث إلى المدعي بقولهم : مات وتركه ميراثاً له... إلى آخره، خلافاً لأبي يوسف الله فإنَّه لا يشترط عنده الجرّ. (فإن قال : كان لأبيه إعارة أو أودعه أو اجاره مَن في يدِهِ جاز بلا جر) ؛
لأن يد المستعير، والمودع ، والمستأجر قائمة مقام يده فلا حاجة إلى الجرّ . [1] قوله : ولزم الجر...الخ ؛ يعني إذا ادعى الوارث شيئاً في يد إنسان أنه ميراث أبيه مثلاً، وشهد الشاهدان أنَّ هذا كان لأبيه لا يقضي له حتى يجرّ الميراث حقيقة، بأن يقول الشاهد: مات وتركه ميراثاً للمدعي ، أو حكماً بأن يقول : مات وهذا ملكه، أو في يده، أو في تصرفه. أما إذا قال : كان لأبيه، لا تقبل شهادته ؛ لعدم الجر حقيقة وحكماً، وهذا عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف له : تقبل شهادته بلا جرّ ؛ لأن ملك المورث كالوارث ، لكون الوراثة خلافة ؛ ولهذا يردُّ بالعيب، ويرد عليه بـه ، فصارت الشهادة بالملكِ للموت شهادة الوارث. ولهما : إنَّ ملك الوارث يتجدَّدُ في الأعيان، وإن لم يتجدد في حق الديون ؛ ولهذا يجب الاستبراء على الوارث في الجارية الموروثة ، ويحل للوارث الغني ما كان صدقة على المورث الفقير والمتجدد يحتاج إلى النقل، فلا يكون استصحاب المال مثبتاً ، لكن يكتفى بالشهادة على قيام ملك المورث وقت الموت ؛ لثبوت الانتقال حينئذ ضرورة.
وكذا الشهادة على قيام يده ؛ لأنَّ الأيدي عند الموت تنقلب يد ملك بواسطة الضمان، إذ الظاهرُ من حال المسلم في ذلك الوقت أن يسوِّي أسبابه ويبين ما كان من الودائع والغصوب، فإذا لم يبين، فالظاهرُ من حالِهِ أن ما في يده ملكه ، فجَعَلَ اليد عند الموتِ دليلُ الملك. كذا في «الدرر»، و«العناية»(۳).
Section on testimony regarding inheritance. And linking (al-jarr) is required from the witness for inheritance by his statement: 'He died and left it as an inheritance for him', or 'He died and this was in his ownership', or 'in his hand'. But if he said: 'It was his father's as a loan, or deposit, or lease from the one in whose hand it was', it is permissible without linking. [1] 'And linking is required from the witness for inheritance by his statement: He died and left it as an inheritance for him, or He died and this was in his ownership, or in his hand.' Meaning: When the witnesses say that this deceased (the testator) owned this thing claimed, the heir is not adjudged until they link the inheritance to the claimant by saying: 'He died and left it as an inheritance for him', etc. Contrary to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, for he does not require linking. (If he said: 'It was his father's as a loan, or deposit, or lease from the one in whose hand it was', it is permissible without linking) because the hand of the borrower, depositee, and lessee stands in place of his hand, so there is no need for linking. [1] His statement: 'And linking is required...' Means: If the heir claims something in someone's hand that it is, for example, his father's inheritance, and the two witnesses testify that this was his father's, the judge does not decide in his favor until they link the inheritance actually, such as the witness saying: 'He died and left it as an inheritance for the claimant', or legally by saying: 'He died and this was his ownership, or in his hand, or in his disposition.' As for if he said: 'It was his father's', his testimony is not accepted due to the lack of linking actually and legally. And this is according to the two [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?] and Abu Yusuf said: His testimony is accepted without linking because the ownership of the testator is like the heir's due to inheritance being a succession; hence he can return due to defect and it is returned to him, so testimony to ownership for the deceased becomes testimony for the heir. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?): The ownership of the heir is renewed in specific properties, even if not renewed regarding debts; hence istibra' is required for the heir regarding the inherited slave-girl, and it becomes permissible for the wealthy heir what was charity for the poor testator. And what is renewed needs transfer, so istishab of property is not probative. However, testimony to the existence of the testator's ownership at the time of death is sufficient due to the necessity of transfer at that time. Likewise, testimony to the existence of his hand (possession) because at the time of death, hands are converted into ownership by way of guarantee, since the apparent condition of a Muslim at that time is that he settles his affairs and clarifies whatever was deposits or usurpations. So if he did not clarify, the apparent from his condition is that what is in his hand is his ownership. Thus the hand at death is made evidence of ownership. As in 'al-Durar' and 'al-'Inayah' (reference 3).
Source pages p. 599

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the requirement of 'al-jarr' (linking) in testimony for inheritance. The author (likely al-Marghinani or another commentator) explains that when a witness testifies that a particular property belonged to the deceased, the judge does not award it to the heir unless the witness explicitly links the property to the heir by saying, for example, 'He died and left it as an inheritance for him,' or 'He died and this was his ownership.' The mere statement 'This was his father's' is insufficient because it does not establish the transfer of ownership from the deceased to the heir. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (the two shaykhs), while Imam Abu Yusuf holds that such testimony is sufficient without explicit linking. Abu Yusuf argues that the testator's ownership is like the heir's due to the nature of inheritance as succession; thus, testimony to the deceased's ownership is effectively testimony for the heir. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) respond that the heir's ownership is newly established in the specific property, not simply a continuation. They cite evidence such as the requirement of istibra' for an inherited slave-girl and the permission for a wealthy heir to benefit from what was charity for the poor testator. Since ownership is renewed, it requires transfer, and istishab (presumption of continuity) does not suffice. However, they do accept testimony to the existence of the testator's ownership or possession at the time of death, because at death, the presumption is that the person has settled accounts, so possession then indicates ownership. The exception is when the property was in the father's hand as a loan, deposit, or lease; there, no linking is needed because the father's possession is already representative.

Key Terms
الجر (al-jarr): Linking or connecting the inheritance to the heir explicitly in testimony, such as saying 'He died and left it as an inheritance for him'.
يد (yad): Hand/possession; physical control over property. At time of death, possession is presumed to be ownership due to the likelihood of settling affairs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding inheritance testimony?
A) Whether the witness must be male.
B) Whether explicit linking (al-jarr) is required.
C) Whether the property must be valued.
D) Whether the heir must be present.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, inheritance testimony requires explicit linking (al-jarr) to the heir; without it, the testimony is rejected, except when the property was held by the deceased as a loan, deposit, or lease.

Shahadat — Testimony

8 min
Testimony on a Living Person's Past Possession is Rejected
Main ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi ruling on testimony about a living person's past possession and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute with Imam Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A living person's hand is ambiguous — testimony of past possession alone cannot prove ownership.

This lesson focuses on a key ruling in Hanafi testimony law: when witnesses testify that an object was in the hand of a living claimant at a past time, the testimony is rejected. The passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold this view, while Abu Yusuf accepts such testimony. Understanding the reasoning behind the majority position is essential for correctly applying the rules of evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Locate the main statement: 'ولو شهدا بيد حي منذ كذا ردت'. Understand that the ruling concerns testimony about past possession of a living person, not a deceased person.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The hand (yad) of a living person is ambiguous: it could indicate ownership, custody, deposit, or usurpation. Testimony does not resolve this ambiguity, so it is rejected.
Key: for a deceased, there is a presumption of ownership at death, which removes ambiguity.
3
Compare the opposing view (Abu Yusuf)
Abu Yusuf accepts such testimony by analogy to the deceased case. He argues that the hand generally indicates ownership.
The majority response: the presumption at death is unique and not transferable to a living person.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the school is rejection, as stated by Imam al-Tumurtashi and affirmed in later commentaries.
Fatwa is on the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شهدا بيد حي منذ كذا ردت
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
(ولو شهدا بيد حي منذ كذا ردت) : أي شهدا أنَّه كان في يدِ المدَّعى" منذ شهر
الموتِ دليلُ الملك. كذا في «الدرر»، و«العناية»(۳).
[٤]
وقال في «المنح»(۳) : ولا بُدَّ مع الجرِّ المذكور من بيان سبب الوراثة، وإذا شهدوا أنه أخوه فلا بد من بيان أنه أخوه لأبيه وأمه ، أو لأحدهما، صرح به في «البحر»)، وغيره، وكذا إذا شهدوا أنه عمه أو مولاه ، لم تقبل ؛ لأنَّ المولى مشترك، فإن قالوا : هو مولاه أعتقه، ولا نعلم له وارثاً غيره فحينئذ تقبل. انتهى.
وقال في «الفتح») : وينسبا الميت والوارث حتى يلتقيا إلى أب واحد، ويذكر أنه وارثه، وهل يشترط قوله : ووارثه في الأب والأم والولد، قيل: يشترط، والفتوى على عدمه، و وكذا كلُّ مَن لا يحجب بحال، وفي الشهادة بأنه ابن ابن الميت أو بنت ابنه لا بدَّ منه، وفي أنه مولاه لا بُدَّ من بيان أنه أعتقه. انتهى.
[١]قوله : ولو شهدا...الخ ؛ صورته : إنَّ الدار مثلاً كانت في يد رجل فادعاها رجل آخر أنها له، وليست الدَّارُ عند الدعوى في يده ، وشهد الشاهدان أنها كانت في يد المدعي منذ شهر مثلاً لا تقبل شهادتهما عند الطرفين، وعند أبي يوسف له تقبل. (۲) قوله : حى ؛ قيد به ؛ لأنَّ الشاهدين إذا شهدا للميت بأن هذه الدار مثلاً كانت
في يده وقت الموت تقبلُ بالإجماع وتكون الدار لوارثه، وقد تقدم ذكره. (۳) قوله : في يد المدعي ؛ قيّد به ؛ لأنه إذا شهدا أنه كان للمدعي لم تقبل
بالاتفاق.
[٤]قوله : منذ شهر ؛ ليس بقيد، فإنَّ الخلاف ثابت فيما لم يذكره، فإنّه ذكر الإمامُ التُمُرْتَاشِيُّ : لو شهدوا لحي أنَّ العينَ كانت في يده لم تقبل ؛ لأنَّ اليد محتملة يد غصب أو يد ملك ، فإذا كانت يد غصب عن ذي اليد لا تجب إعادته، وإن كانت يد ملك تجب، فلا تجب بالشك. كذا في «جامع الفصولين».
And if two witnesses testify about the hand of a living person since such-and-such a time, it is rejected. (And if two witnesses testify about the hand of a living person since such-and-such a time, it is rejected): meaning they testify that it was in the hand of the claimant since a month. (reference to previous line). And it is said in 'al-Manh' (3): There must be, along with the aforementioned linking, a statement of the cause of inheritance. And if they testify that he is his brother, then it is necessary to clarify that he is his brother from both father and mother, or from one of them. This is explicitly stated in 'al-Bahr' and others. Likewise, if they testify that he is his uncle or his master (mawla), it is not accepted because 'mawla' is ambiguous. However, if they say: He is his master who freed him, and we do not know any other heir for him, then it is accepted. End. And it is said in 'al-Fath' (5): They should attribute the deceased and the heir until they meet at one father, and he mentions that he is his heir. And is it required to say 'and his heir' regarding the father, mother, and child? It is said it is required, but the fatwa is against it. Likewise, everyone who is never blocked (by hajb) is like that. And in testimony that he is the son of the son of the deceased or the daughter of his son, it is indispensable. And regarding that he is his mawla, it is necessary to clarify that he freed him. End. [1] His statement: 'And if two witnesses testify...' etc. Its scenario: Suppose the house was in the hand of a man, and another man claims it is his, and the house is not in his hand at the time of the claim. The two witnesses testify that it was in the hand of the claimant since a month, for example. Their testimony is not accepted according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), while according to Abu Yusuf, it is accepted. (2) His statement: 'living' — it is specified because if the two witnesses testify for the deceased that this house, for example, was in his hand at the time of death, it is accepted by consensus, and the house belongs to his heir, as previously mentioned. (3) His statement: 'in the hand of the claimant' — it is specified because if they testify that it belonged to the claimant, it is not accepted by agreement. [4] His statement: 'since a month' — it is not a restriction, for the disagreement applies even if they do not mention a time. Indeed, Imam al-Tumurtashi said: If they testify for a living person that the object was in his hand, it is not accepted because the hand could be a hand of usurpation or a hand of ownership. If it is a hand of usurpation, returning it is not obligatory; if it is a hand of ownership, returning it is obligatory. So it is not obligatory by doubt. As in 'Jami' al-Fusulayn'.
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage addresses two connected issues: testimony about a living person's past possession and the requirement to specify the cause of inheritance in testimony. The first ruling is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning testimony that an object was in the possession of a living claimant at some earlier time. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, such testimony is rejected because possession (yad) alone is ambiguous—it could be ownership or merely custody (such as a loan or deposit). At the time of death, this ambiguity is resolved by a legal presumption, but for a living person, no such presumption exists. Imam Abu Yusuf accepts such testimony by analogy to the deceased case. The majority position is the relied-upon view. The second part, derived from al-Manh and al-Fath, clarifies that in inheritance testimony, the witnesses must specify the exact cause of kinship—for example, not merely 'brother' but 'brother from both parents or from one parent.' For a 'mawla' (patron/freed slave), they must state that he freed him. For distant relatives like grandchildren, explicit lineage must be traced. This ensures the court can identify the correct heir and avoid ambiguity. The fatwa in some cases—like testimony for parents and children—does not require the phrase 'and his heir' because such relationships are universally recognized.

Key Terms
شهادة باليد (shahadah bil-yad): Testimony that a person had physical possession of an item at a specific time, used to claim ownership.
يد (yad): Hand; legally, possession or control. Can be ownership, custody, or usurpation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is testimony about a living person's past possession rejected according to the majority Hanafi view?
A) Because the witness must also state the claimant is the owner.
B) Because a living person's hand could be custody or usurpation, not necessarily ownership.
C) Because testimony must be about possession at the time of death.
D) Because the judge does not accept testimony about past events.

Key Takeaway
Testimony about a living person's past possession is rejected due to the ambiguity of the hand, with fatwa on the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Specifying the Cause of Inheritance in Testimony
Ruling on specifying inheritance cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the requirements for specifying the cause of inheritance in testimony and identify which relationships require explicit clarification.

Core Idea
In inheritance testimony, witnesses must specify the precise legal relationship to avoid ambiguity.

This lesson addresses a second crucial point from the passage: when witnesses testify to establish inheritance, they must clarify the exact cause (sabab) of inheritance—for example, not just 'brother' but 'brother from both parents'. The passage from al-Manh and al-Fath details the requirements and exceptions, providing essential guidelines for drafting valid inheritance testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage states: 'ولا بد مع الجر المذكور من بيان سبب الوراثة' – there must be a statement of the cause of inheritance.
2
Apply to specific relationships
For 'brother', it is not enough; must specify 'brother from both parents' or 'from one parent'. Similarly for 'uncle' or 'mawla'.
The term 'mawla' is ambiguous; must state 'he freed him'.
3
Understand the nuance in paternal relationships
For father, mother, and child, the fatwa is that stating 'he is his heir' is not required because these relationships are unambiguous and never blocked.
But for grandchildren (son's son/daughter), explicit attribution is necessary.
4
Trace the lineage requirement
Al-Fath requires connecting the deceased and heir up to a common ancestor and mentioning that the witness is the heir.
5
Identify exceptions and the wisdom
The rule prevents fraud and ensures the court correctly identifies the heir. Ambiguous terms like 'mawla' or distant relations require extra detail.
The fatwa waives the phrase 'and his heir' for near, unblocked relatives.

(3) His statement: 'in the hand of the claimant' — it is specified because if they testify that it belonged to the claimant, it is not accepted by agreement. [4] His statement: 'since a month' — it is not a restriction, for the disagreement applies even if they do not mention a time. Indeed, Imam al-Tumurtashi said: If they testify for a living person that the object was in his hand, it is not accepted because the hand could be a hand of usurpation or a hand of ownership. If it is a hand of usurpation, returning it is not obligatory; if it is a hand of ownership, returning it is obligatory. So it is not obligatory by doubt. As in 'Jami' al-Fusulayn'.
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage addresses two connected issues: testimony about a living person's past possession and the requirement to specify the cause of inheritance in testimony. The first ruling is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning testimony that an object was in the possession of a living claimant at some earlier time. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, such testimony is rejected because possession (yad) alone is ambiguous—it could be ownership or merely custody (such as a loan or deposit). At the time of death, this ambiguity is resolved by a legal presumption, but for a living person, no such presumption exists. Imam Abu Yusuf accepts such testimony by analogy to the deceased case. The majority position is the relied-upon view. The second part, derived from al-Manh and al-Fath, clarifies that in inheritance testimony, the witnesses must specify the exact cause of kinship—for example, not merely 'brother' but 'brother from both parents or from one parent.' For a 'mawla' (patron/freed slave), they must state that he freed him. For distant relatives like grandchildren, explicit lineage must be traced. This ensures the court can identify the correct heir and avoid ambiguity. The fatwa in some cases—like testimony for parents and children—does not require the phrase 'and his heir' because such relationships are universally recognized.

Key Terms
سبب الوراثة (sabab al-wirathah): The legal cause of inheritance, such as being a brother, uncle, or freed slave, which must be specified in testimony.
مولى (mawla): A patron or freed slave; ambiguous term that can mean master or freed person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when witnesses testify that a person is the 'mawla' of the deceased, what additional information is required?
A) They must state the mawla's full name.
B) They must state that the deceased freed him and that there is no other heir.
C) They must state the exact monetary value of any gift.
D) They must state that the mawla is from the same tribe.

Key Takeaway
In inheritance testimony, witnesses must specify the exact cause of inheritance; ambiguous relationships require clarification to ensure correct distribution.

Shahadat — Testimony

Testimony on Possession of a Living Person and Iqrar
Intra-Hanafi dispute on testimony of possession and iqrar rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi majority rejects testimony of a living person's possession and apply the rule that testimony based on iqrar is accepted.

Core Idea
Possession is ambiguous; testimony about it is rejected unless based on a clear admission.

In Hanafi fiqh, possession (yad) can indicate ownership, trust, or liability, making it ambiguous. When witnesses testify that a living person had possession of an object in the past, the majority of the school rejects such testimony because the court cannot determine the type of possession. However, if the defendant himself admits (iqrar) that the object was in the claimant's possession, testimony about that admission is valid. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute on this issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
The issue is whether testimony that a living person had possession of an object for a past period is acceptable. The text states that such testimony is 'rejected' (ردت) according to the majority, but Abu Yusuf accepts it.
الرد means the testimony is not considered valid proof.
2
Analyze the majority's position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue that possession is ambiguous (متنوعة) – it could be ownership, trust, or liability. The court cannot adjudicate based on uncertainty, so the testimony is rejected.
They rely on the principle that certainty is required for judgment.
3
Understand Abu Yusuf's counterargument
Abu Yusuf draws an analogy (qiyas) to ownership: if testimony of past ownership is accepted, why not past possession? He considers possession prima facie evidence of ownership.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
4
Evaluate the majority's response
The majority refutes the analogy by showing a difference (qiyas ma'a al-fariq): ownership is a single known concept, whereas possession is divided into types. Thus, analogizing possession to ownership is invalid.
The response highlights the importance of the 'illa (effective cause) being consistent.
5
Distinguish the iqrar scenario
If the defendant admits (iqrar) that the object was in the claimant's possession, or witnesses testify to that admission, the testimony is accepted regardless of possession's ambiguity. Iqrar is a clear legal act; ignorance of the confessed matter's details does not invalidate it.
Iqrar is binding evidence against the confessor, making the testimony reliable.

And if two witnesses testify concerning the possession of a living person from such a time, it is rejected. Summary of al-Nihayah for 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah: (And if two witnesses testify concerning the possession of a living person from such a time, it is rejected): i.e., they testify that it was in the possession of the defendant from a month, death is evidence of ownership. As in al-Durar and al-‘Inayah. [4] And he said in al-Minah: And there must be with the mentioned dragging an explanation of the cause of inheritance, and if they testify that he is his brother, then there must be an explanation that he is his brother from father and mother, or from one of them, as stated in al-Bahr and others. Likewise, if they testify that he is his uncle or his mawla, it is not accepted because al-mawla is ambiguous; if they say: he is his mawla, he freed him, and we know of no other heir for him, then it is accepted. End. And he said in al-Fath: And they should trace the deceased and the heir until they meet at one father, and mention that he is his heir. And is it required that he say 'and his heir' regarding the father, mother, and child? It is said it is required, and the fatwa is against it. [1] His statement: And if two witnesses... etc.; its scenario: If a house, for example, was in the possession of a man, and another man claims it is his, and the house is not in his possession at the time of the claim, and the two witnesses testify that it was in the possession of the claimant for a month, for example, their testimony is not accepted according to both sides, and according to Abu Yusuf it is accepted. (2) His statement: 'living' is a restriction because if the two witnesses testify for the deceased that this house, for example, was in his possession at the time of death, it is accepted by consensus and the house goes to his heir, as previously mentioned. (3) His statement: 'in the possession of the claimant' is a restriction because if they testify that it belonged to the claimant, it is not accepted by agreement. [4] His statement: 'from a month' is not a restriction, because the dispute exists even if he did not mention it. Indeed, Imam al-Tumurtashi said: If they testify for a living person that the object was in his possession, it is not accepted because possession is potentially possession by usurpation or possession of ownership. If it is possession by usurpation, returning it to the possessor is not obligatory, and if it is possession of ownership, it is obligatory, so it is not obligatory due to doubt. As in Jami' al-Fusulayn. 'And if the defendant admits to that, or if two witnesses testify that he admitted to possession of the claimant, it is valid': while it is not in the possession of the claimant at the time of the claim, it is not accepted because possession is of various types: possession of ownership, possession of trust, and possession of liability, so adjudging the return of the unknown is difficult. And according to Abu Yusuf, it is accepted. (And if the defendant admits to that, or if two witnesses testify that he admitted to possession of the claimant, it is valid) because ignorance of the thing confessed does not prevent the validity of the admission. [1] His statement: 'it is accepted' because possession is as significant as ownership, and when ownership is established, it remains until something removes it, similarly for possession. If they testify that it was his ownership from a month, it is accepted, so likewise this. And it is as if they testify that it was in the possession of the claimant and the defendant, who is the possessor, took it, it is accepted. The answer to this analogy is that ownership is known and not different, even if its causes are various, and the taking is also known by testimony, and its ruling is also known, which is the obligation of return, unlike possession, for it is broken and ambiguous because it is divided into types previously indicated. And analogizing the unknown to the known is an analogy with a difference. [2] His statement: 'And if he admits...' etc.; meaning if the defendant admits to possession for the claimant, or two witnesses testify that he admitted to possession for the claimant from a month, for example, it is valid, and that is given to the claimant because the admission is known, so the testimony about it is valid, and ignorance of the thing confessed does not prevent the validity of the admission. Do you not see that if he said: 'I owe something to so-and-so,' it is valid, and he is required to explain, but testimony about it is not valid.
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage addresses two scenarios in testimony (shahada) regarding possession (yad). The first scenario: witnesses testify that a living person had possession of an object for a period (e.g., one month). The ruling according to the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) is that such testimony is rejected because possession can be of various types – ownership, trust, or liability – and the uncertainty prevents adjudication. However, Abu Yusuf holds that it is accepted, analogizing possession to ownership: if testimony of ownership from a month is accepted, so should testimony of possession. The Hanafi majority refutes this by noting that ownership is a single clear concept, whereas possession is ambiguous and divided into types; thus the analogy is flawed (qiyas ma'a al-fariq). The second scenario: if the defendant confesses (iqrar) that the object was in the claimant's possession, or witnesses testify to such a confession, the testimony is accepted. This is because iqrar is a clear legal act; ignorance of the precise nature of the confessed matter does not invalidate the iqrar itself. However, testimony about that iqrar requires the iqrar to be known, not the underlying details. The passage also discusses requirements for specifying kinship in inheritance testimony.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Physical possession or control; in legal context, it can denote ownership (yad milk), trust (yad amanah), or liability (yad daman).
شهادة (shahada): Testimony given by two witnesses in court to establish a fact.
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession by a party; it is binding evidence against the confessor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The testimony is accepted by consensus.
B) The testimony is rejected due to the ambiguous nature of possession.
C) The testimony is accepted only if the claimant is the defendant.
D) The testimony is accepted only for deceased persons.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority rejects testimony of a living person's possession due to ambiguity, but testimony based on a clear iqrar (admission) is accepted.

Testimony on Possession vs. Acknowledgment: Certainty and Ambiguity
Commentary on testimony about possession and acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings on testimony about possession versus testimony about acknowledgment and evaluate the qiyas used by Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Possession is ambiguous, so testimony fails; acknowledgment is clear, so testimony succeeds.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the acceptance of testimony in property claims. It contrasts testimony about past possession (which Abu Hanifa rejects due to ambiguity) with testimony about acknowledgment (which is accepted). The commentator then defends Abu Hanifa's view against a qiyas argued by Abu Yusuf, demonstrating why the analogy between ownership and possession fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues
The passage addresses two distinct scenarios: (1) testimony that the property was in the plaintiff's hand (possession) at the time of claim, and (2) the effect of the defendant's acknowledgment or testimony about that acknowledgment.
The first scenario triggers a dispute; the second is uncontroversial.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that testimony about past possession is not accepted when the plaintiff did not have possession at the time of claim, because possession is ambiguous. Abu Yusuf disagrees and accepts such testimony.
This is an ikhtilaf within the school, not between madhhabs.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's qiyas
Abu Yusuf analogizes possession to ownership: if testimony about past ownership is accepted, so should testimony about past possession. The analogy is based on the idea that both are knowable states.
The qiyas is jaliy (clear analogy) but rebutted.
4
Grasp the Hanafi response to the qiyas
The commentator refutes the qiyas by pointing out that ownership is a stable legal status invariant to its causes, whereas possession is divided into multiple types (ownership, trust, guarantee) making it ambiguous. Hence, the analogy is qiyas ma'a al-fariq (analogy with a difference).
This critique preserves the principle that uncertain matters cannot form the basis of testimony.

Book of Testimony and Retraction Therefrom: Disputes in Testimony. And if the defendant acknowledges that, or two witnesses testify that he acknowledged the plaintiff's possession, it is valid. [1] And when there is no possession in the plaintiff's hand at the time of the claim, it is not accepted, because possession is divided into ownership possession, trust possession, and guarantee possession, so judgment becomes difficult due to unknown factors. According to Abu Yusuf, it is accepted. (And if the defendant acknowledges that, or two witnesses testify that he acknowledged the plaintiff's possession, it is valid) because ignorance of what is acknowledged does not prevent the validity of the acknowledgment. [1] His statement: 'It is accepted' because possession is intended like ownership, and when ownership is established, it remains until something removes it, so too with possession. If they testify that it was his ownership since a month, it is accepted; similarly this. And it becomes like when they testify that it was in the plaintiff's hand, and the defendant who is the possessor took it, it is accepted. The response to this analogy: Ownership is known and not varied, although its causes are diverse; the taking is also known by testimony, and its ruling is known—namely, the obligation to return—unlike possession, which is subject to variation because it is divided into types previously indicated. Comparing the unknown to the known is an analogy with a difference. [2] His statement: 'And if he acknowledges...' etc.; meaning if the defendant acknowledges possession to the plaintiff, or two witnesses testify that he acknowledged possession to the plaintiff since a month, for example, it is valid, and that possession is handed to the plaintiff because the acknowledgment is known, so testimony based on it is valid. Ignorance of what is acknowledged does not prevent the validity of acknowledgment. Do you not see that if he said: 'So-and-so has something due to me,' it is valid, and the explanation is left to him, but testimony based on it is not valid.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn regarding testimony in claims. It addresses two points: (1) testimony that a property was in the plaintiff's hand (possession) at the time of claim, and (2) the effect of the defendant's acknowledgment or witness testimony that the defendant acknowledged the plaintiff's possession. The commentator explains that testimony about possession when the plaintiff did not actually have possession at the time of claim is not accepted because possession is ambiguous—it can be of many types (ownership, trust, guarantee). Thus, it is not a sufficient basis for judgment. However, Abu Yusuf holds that such testimony is accepted. The commentator then addresses a qiyas (analogy) that compares possession to ownership: if testimony about past ownership from a month ago is accepted, so should testimony about past possession. The response is that ownership is a stable legal status unaffected by cause, whereas possession is variable; therefore, the analogy is invalid. The second point is that if the defendant acknowledges the plaintiff's possession, or if witnesses testify that the defendant made such an acknowledgment, it is valid, because the acknowledgment is a known act. Ignorance of the details (what exactly was acknowledged) does not invalidate the acknowledgment, though such testimony would not be accepted for an unspecified debt.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Possession or control over property, which can be of various types (ownership, trust, guarantee).
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right or fact by a party, which is considered a clear legal basis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Abu Hanifa reject testimony that a property was in the plaintiff's hand a month ago when the plaintiff did not have possession at the time of claim?
A) Because all testimony about past events is rejected
B) Because possession is ambiguous — it can be ownership, trust, or guarantee possession
C) Because the defendant's acknowledgment is required
D) Because Abu Yusuf's opinion is stronger

Key Takeaway
Testimony about past possession is not accepted due to ambiguity, but testimony about an acknowledgment of possession is valid because the acknowledgment itself is a definite legal act.

Secondary Testimony: Acceptance by Istihsan
Commentary on the matn allowing secondary testimony with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on secondary testimony, explain why qiyas rejects it and istihsan permits it, and identify the exceptions (hudud, qisas) and the intra-Hanafi dispute on tazir.

Core Idea
Secondary testimony is accepted by istihsan to protect rights, even though qiyas would forbid it.

This passage addresses the acceptance of testimony based on another witness's testimony (شهادة على الشهادة). While analogy (qiyas) would reject it because testifying is a personal act of worship, the Hanafi school permits it by istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent loss of rights when the original witness cannot appear. The ruling is general for all rights, with exceptions for fixed punishments (hudud) and retaliation (qisas), and there is a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school regarding discretionary punishments (tazir).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its apparent proof
The matn states that secondary testimony is accepted except in hadd and qisas. Recognize that this is the operative ruling.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
The commentator explains that by qiyas, secondary testimony should be invalid because testifying is a bodily act of worship (عبادة بدنية) that cannot be delegated, and it increases probability of error.
This is the objection that istihsan overrides.
3
Analyze the istihsan justification
Istihsan permits it because if the original witness faces impediments, rights would be lost. Secondary testimony serves as a substitute.
Key principle: preventing loss of rights outweighs the qiyas objection.
4
Examine the exception and its rationale
Secondary testimony is not accepted in hudud and qisas because these are dropped by doubts (شبهات). The principle: matters that are warded off by doubts do not accept weaker evidence.
5
Distinguish the intra-Hanafi dispute on tazir
Abu Hanifa excludes tazir (like hudud), while Abu Yusuf includes it because tazir is not invalidated by doubt, citing the Prophet's act of imprisoning on suspicion.
The text does not specify the mufta bihi; modern practice often follows Abu Yusuf given the hadith evidence.

Source Text

Chapter on Testimony Based on Testimony. And testimony based on testimony is accepted except in hadd and qisas. Chapter on Testimony Based on Testimony. [1] And testimony based on testimony is accepted except in hadd and qisas. [1] His statement: 'And testimony based on testimony is accepted' — this is the beginning of the rulings of secondary testimony after finishing with the rulings of primary testimony. It was placed after primary testimony because the secondary requires the primary. The analogy (qiyas) rejects its permissibility, because the performance is a bodily act of worship obligatory upon the original witness, and it is not a right of the person for whom testimony is given, since litigation and compulsion are not permitted in it. Bodily acts of worship do not admit substitution, and because it involves increased probability, since the probability occurs in two places: in the original and in the secondary. However, it was permitted by istihsan because the original witness may encounter impediments that prevent attendance; if secondary testimony were not accepted, rights would be lost. It is a substitute for the original testimony. If you say: If there were in them the meaning of substitution, then combining them would not be allowed, since combination between the substitute and the substituted is not allowed, but if one of the two witnesses testifies as an original, and two others testify based on the testimony of another witness, it is permitted. I say: The substitution is only in the matter testified to; the matter testified to by the secondary is the testimony of the original, and the matter testified to by the original is what they witnessed of the plaintiff's claim. If so, the secondary's testimony is not based on the original's testimony, so it is not prevented that the original be completed by the secondary. And when substitution is established in it, it is not accepted in matters that are dropped by doubts, like the testimony of women with men. Thus it is accepted in all rights like property, and in the case of waqf it is correct to preserve it and protect it from being lost, and in tazir. As stated in al-Bahr, quoting from al-Ajnas. Mentioned in al-Ikhtiyar: What necessitates tazir, according to Abu Hanifa, is not accepted like other punishments; according to Abu Yusuf, it is accepted because tazir is not dropped by doubt, as transmitted that the Prophet imprisoned a man on suspicion.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn stating that testimony based on another's testimony (شهادة على شهادة) is accepted except in cases of hadd (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation). The commentator explains the reasoning: according to qiyas (analogy), secondary testimony should not be allowed because testifying is a bodily act of worship that cannot be delegated. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), it is allowed because otherwise rights might be lost if the original witness cannot appear. The commentator responds to an objection that substitution and substitute cannot be combined: the substitution is in the content (the original testimony), not in the act of testifying. Therefore, it is permissible to complete the evidence with secondary testimony. The passage then discusses exceptions: secondary testimony is not accepted in matters that are dropped by doubts, such as hudud and qisas. For tazir (discretionary punishment), there is a difference: Abu Hanifa says it is not accepted like other punishments, but Abu Yusuf says it is accepted because tazir is not invalidated by doubt, citing the Prophet's imprisonment on suspicion. The relied-upon position is not explicitly stated here.

Key Terms
شهادة على الشهادة (shahāda 'alā al-shahāda): Secondary testimony: witnessing based on the testimony of another witness, not direct observation.
تعزير (taʿzīr): Discretionary punishment determined by the judge for offenses not subject to fixed hadd punishment.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; preferring a ruling that deviates from qiyas due to necessity or public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is secondary testimony (شهادة على الشهادة) not accepted in cases of hadd and qisas?
A) Because secondary testimony is inherently less reliable than primary testimony
B) Because hudud and qisas are dropped by doubts, and secondary testimony introduces additional doubt
C) Because the Prophet prohibited it for serious punishments
D) Because the original witness must always appear personally for hudud

Key Takeaway
Secondary testimony is permitted by istihsan to preserve rights, but not for hudud and qisas; for tazir, the Hanafi school has an internal disagreement.

Ruling on Testimony on Testimony and Its Exception
States ruling with istihsan proof and exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shahada 'ala al-shahada, explain its istihsan justification, and identify the exception for hudud/qisas and the dispute on tazir.

Core Idea
Testimony on testimony accepted by istihsan to preserve rights, but not in hudud or qisas; disputed for tazir.

This passage from Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the ruling on shahada 'ala al-shahada (testimony on testimony). Normally, qiyas would reject it because testimony is a bodily act of worship that cannot be deputized. However, the Hanafi school accepts it by istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent the loss of rights when original witnesses are unavailable. The ruling includes an exception for hadd punishments and qisas, and an intra-Hanafi difference on tazir (discretionary punishment).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and exception
Read the opening sentence: 'And testimony on testimony is accepted except in hadd and qisas.' Note the general acceptance and the categorical exception.
This is the foundational statement of the chapter.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
The commentary states that qiyas prohibits acceptance because testimony is a bodily act of worship that cannot be performed by proxy, and it increases the chance of error (probability in two places). Comprehend why qiyas would reject branch testimony.
This sets up the necessity for istihsan.
3
Grasp the istihsan justification
The text says that if branch testimony were not accepted, rights would be lost due to impediments affecting the original witness (death, travel, etc.). Istihsan overrides qiyas for public interest.
The maslaha (preservation of rights) is the basis.
4
Analyze the substitution (badaliyya) concept
The passage addresses an objection: if branch testimony is a substitute, combining original and branch should be invalid. The response clarifies that the substitution is in the ‘subject matter’—branch witnesses testify to the original's testimony, not directly to the event. Thus, combining is permissible.
This defends the validity of having some original witnesses and some branch witnesses in the same case.
5
Examine the dispute on tazir
The text reports two views: Abu Hanifa excludes tazir from acceptance (like hudud), while Abu Yusuf includes it, because tazir is not dropped by doubts (citing the Prophet’s imprisonment on suspicion). Compare the proofs and practical consequences.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with no tarjih given here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter on testimony on testimony. And testimony on testimony is accepted except in hadd and qisas. [1] His statement: 'And testimony on testimony is accepted' — this is the beginning of the rulings of branch testimony after finishing the rulings of original testimony. It was delayed after original testimony because the branch requires the original to precede. Analogy (qiyas) prevents its permissibility because performance is a bodily act of worship obligated upon the original witness, and it is not a right for the plaintiff because litigation and compulsion are not permissible in it. A bodily act of worship does not allow deputyship. And because there is increased probability, for probability exists in two places: in the origins and in the branches. However, it is permitted by istihsan because there may occur to the original witness impediments that prevent attendance. If branch testimony were not accepted, rights would be lost. It is a substitute for original testimony. If you say: If there were bodily meaning in both, combining them would not be permitted, for combination between the substitute and the substituted is not permitted; but if one of the two witnesses testified as an original, and two others testify on the testimony of another witness, it is permitted. I say: Bodiliness is only in the testified matter. What is testified by branch testimony is the testimony of the original; what is testified by original testimony is what they witnessed of what the plaintiff claims. If so, branch testimony is not about the original's testimony; thus completion of the original by the branch is not prevented. When substitution is established, it is not accepted in matters that are dropped by doubts, like the testimony of women with men. So it is accepted in all rights such as property, and endowment (waqf) according to the correct view as a revival of it and preservation from its extinction, and tazir. As stated in al-Bahr, citing al-Ajnas. And it is mentioned in al-Ikhtiyar: What necessitates tazir, according to Abu Hanifa, is not accepted like other punishments. According to Abu Yusuf, it is accepted because tazir is not dropped by doubt, due to the narration that the Prophet (peace be upon him) imprisoned a man on suspicion.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on testimony on testimony (shahada 'ala al-shahada) by stating the ruling: it is accepted except in cases of hadd punishment and qisas (retaliation). The commentary then explains the reasoning behind this ruling. Normally, by qiyas (analogy), testimony on testimony would not be accepted because testimony is a bodily act of worship that cannot be deputized. However, the Hanafi school accepts it by istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent the loss of people's rights, as original witnesses may become unable to attend court due to death, illness, or travel. The text addresses a potential objection: if testimony on testimony is a substitute (badal), then combining the original and substitute should be invalid. The response clarifies that the substitutive nature pertains to the subject matter: branch witnesses testify to the original witness's testimony, not directly to the claim. Therefore, combining original and branch testimony is permissible. The exception for hudud and qisas is because these penalties are dropped by mere doubts (shubuhat). The passage then discusses tazir (discretionary punishment): according to Abu Hanifa, testimony on testimony is not accepted in tazir because it is a punishment that falls under the same principle, while Abu Yusuf accepts it, arguing that tazir is not dropped by doubt, citing the Prophet's imprisonment of a suspect. This intra-Hanafi dispute is noted.

Key Terms
شهادة على شهادة (shahāda ʿalā al-shahāda): Testimony given by witnesses who did not witness the original event, but testify to the testimony of the original witnesses (shuhud al-usul).
بدلية (badaliyya): The concept of substitution; branch testimony is a substitute (badal) for original testimony.
تعزير (taʿzīr): Discretionary punishment imposed by the judge for offenses that do not have fixed hadd penalties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is obligatory by Quranic text.
B) Because of istihsan to prevent loss of rights.
C) Because original witnesses are always unreliable.
D) Because it is a form of tawatur.

Key Takeaway
Shahada 'ala al-shahada is a dispensation by istihsan, accepted to protect rights, but excluded in hudud and qisas, and disputed in tazir.

Conditions and Disputes in Testimony on Testimony
States conditions for shahada 'ala al-shahada and intra/cross-madhhab disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for valid testimony on testimony, explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on travel distance, and identify the Hanafi position on the exclusion of hudud.

Core Idea
Testimony on testimony requires the original witness's inability due to death, illness, or travel; hudud are excluded due to doubt.

In this passage, the Hanafi school sets the conditions for accepting branch testimony when the original witness cannot attend. It defines death, severe illness, and travel as valid excuses. The text also presents an intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the required travel distance, and a cross-madhhab disagreement with Imam Malik on whether branch testimony is accepted in hudud cases. The commentary explains each part with legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions
List the three excuses stated in the matn: death, illness (severe enough to prevent attendance), and travel (a distance that qualifies as a travel excuse in Islamic law).
Illness must prevent the witness from reaching the court; minor ailments do not qualify.
2
Examine the definition of travel
The matn implies a full travel distance (safar) sufficient for concessions like qasr. The commentary supports this by analogy with other rulings attached to travel.
Travel is a clear excuse, similar to shortening prayer or breaking fast.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's alternative view
Abu Yusuf considers a distance that prevents the witness from returning home by night as sufficient. This is more lenient and aims to revive people's rights.
The jurists say the matn view is better (ahsan), but Abu Yusuf's is more convenient (arfaq).
4
Review the hudud exclusion
Branch testimony is not accepted in hadd and qisas cases due to doubt. The Hanafi argument refutes Imam Malik's permissibility, citing that hudud are repelled by doubts and branch testimony introduces additional probability of error.
Imam Malik analogized branch testimony to a reliable translator, but Hanafis reject this because the chain adds uncertainty.

(٢) قوله : وعند أبي يوسف الله ; هذا مشعر بكونه مذهب أبي يوسف ، وفي سائر الكتب رواية عنه. [٣] قوله : يكفي ... الخ ; يعني أنَّ الشاهدَ الأصلَ إن كان في مكان لو ذهب بكرةً النهار لأداء الشهادة لا يستطيع بعد الرجوع من مجلس القاضي أن يبيت في أهلِهِ صح الشهادة إحياء لحقوق الناس. قال الفقهاء : الأول ; أي ما ذكر في المتن أحسن ; لأنَّ العذر يتحقق بذلك كما في سائر الأحكام، والثاني : أي ما روي عن أبي يوسف له أرفق ; لأنَّ إحياء الحقوق
And a condition for it is the inability of the original witness to attend due to death, illness, or travel. And according to Abu Yusuf, a distance suffices such that if he goes in the morning, he cannot return to his family by night. [2] And imprisonment is tazir. Thus in al-Munah, except in hadd and qisas, it is not accepted due to doubt. Imam Malik permitted it in hudud because the branches are reliable who transmitted the testimony of the original, so the ruling by the original and branch testimony is like a translator. And our argument: hudud are repelled by doubts, and in testimony on testimony there is doubt due to substitution, or because of increased probability, for every testimony has the probability that the testified matter is not true, and in branch testimony there is another probability, the chance that the original did not exist. [1] His statement: 'And a condition for it is inability...' etc.; because testimony on testimony is not permitted except for need when the original is unable, and inability is realized by these things. The meaning of illness is what prevents him from attending the court, because giving testimony is an obligation, so it is not dropped except by incapacity. When it drops, it becomes permissible for others to bear it so that his right is not lost. Travel is an apparent excuse, for many rulings are attached to it, such as shortening prayer, breaking fast, extending the period of wiping, dropping Jumu'a and sacrifice, and prohibition of a woman leaving without a mahram or husband, and other rulings. Similarly for this ruling. In al-Munah it adds: or if the woman is secluded from the judge's view, as stated in al-Quniya. (2) His statement: 'And according to Abu Yusuf' indicates it is the madhhab of Abu Yusuf, and in other books it is a narration from him. [3] His statement: 'a distance suffices...' etc. means that if the original witness is in a place where if he goes at the beginning of the day to give testimony, he cannot after returning from the judge's court spend the night with his family, the testimony is valid to revive people's rights. The jurists said: the first (what the matn mentioned) is better, because the excuse is realized by that as in other rulings; the second (what is related from Abu Yusuf) is more lenient because reviving rights.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage specifies the conditions required for accepting testimony on testimony. The original witness must be unable to attend court due to death, illness (severe enough to prevent attendance), or travel (a distance that qualifies as a travel excuse in Islamic law). The commentary explains each condition: illness is defined as the inability to reach the court; travel is recognized as a valid excuse similar to other concessions in Islamic law. Abu Yusuf offers a more lenient view: a distance that prevents the witness from returning home by evening suffices, even if not a full travel distance. The jurists consider the first view (the matn's) as better, but Abu Yusuf's as more convenient for people. The passage also reiterates the exception for hudud and qisas, and notes Imam Malik's contrary opinion. The discussion includes a brief refutation of the Maliki view, emphasizing that hudud are dropped by doubts.

Key Terms
تعذر (taʿadhdhur): Inability or impossibility; here, the original witness's inability to attend court.
مرض (maraḍ): Illness that prevents the witness from going to the court.
سفر (safar): Travel; here, a journey that is considered a legitimate excuse in Islamic law.

D) Because the original witness must be present for hudud.

Key Takeaway
Testimony on testimony is a dispensation conditional on the original witness's genuine inability; hudud cases are excluded due to the heightened standard of proof.

The Nisab and Non-Distinctness of Secondary Witnesses in Shahadat al-Far'
Explains the required number and non-distinctness of secondary witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for the number of secondary witnesses (nisab) and explain why the same two witnesses can testify over multiple originals.

Core Idea
Two secondary witnesses suffice over each original; they need not be distinct—removing hardship.

When a primary witness cannot attend court, his testimony may be transmitted through secondary witnesses (shahadat al-far'). The Hanafi school sets conditions for this to balance legal rigor with practical necessity. This lesson unpacks the required number of witnesses and the rule that the same two secondary witnesses may testify over multiple originals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: the condition for secondary testimony. The ruling is that a complete nisab (two men or one man and two women) must testify over each original witness.
The hukm level is wajib (obligatory) as far as possible, but hardship can waive it.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is istihsan (juristic preference) based on removing hardship (haraj) from the witness, as staying overnight away from family is a great hardship. A quote from al-Kubra also supports the nisab condition.
The dalil is not a primary text but a legal reasoning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Istihsan permits secondary testimony to facilitate people's rights. The nisab ensures the testimony meets the standard evidentiary threshold. The permissibility of women completing the nisab is debated; the correct Hanafi view accepts it.
The author cites al-Bahr to refute al-Hawi al-Qudsi's restriction.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars (al-Hawi al-Qudsi) hold that women's testimony is not accepted in secondary testimony. However, al-Bahr and the majority of later scholars accept women if they complete the nisab.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf resolved by tarjih.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The mufta bihi position is that two witnesses (or one man and two women) suffice over multiple originals. The same two secondary witnesses can testify over all originals; distinct pairs are not required.
Al-Barjandi adds an extra condition: the original must be legally capable at the time the secondary testifies.

زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية [۱] وشهادة عدد عن كل أصل لا تغاير فرعي هذا وذاك. واجب ما أمكن، والشاهد أيضاً محتسب، فلا يكلف ما فيه حرج، وفي البيتوتة في غير أهله حرج عظيم، فيجوز الإشهاد على شهادته؛ دفعاً للحرج عنه، وإحياء لحقوق الناس، وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث، وكثير من المشايخ. وروي عن محمد أنه: إن كان الأصل في زاوية المسجد فشهد الفرع على شهادته في زاوية أخرى من ذلك المسجد، تقبل شهادته.
[١] قوله: وشهادة عدد... إلخ؛ أي وشرط لها شهادة عدد نصاب، وهو رجلان أو رجل وامرأتان عن كل أصل؛ لأن الشهادة حق من الحقوق، فلا بد من نصاب الشهادة، قال في «الكبرى»: تقبل فيما لا يسقط بالشبهة إن شهد رجلان على شهادة شاهدين. انتهى. فظاهره أن ذلك شرط فلا تقبل شهادة النساء على الشهادة. وبه صرح في «الحاوي القدسي»، لكن قال في «البحر»: قوله: إن شهد رجلان وقع اتفاقاً؛ لأنه يجوز أن يشهد عليها رجل وامرأتان؛ لتمام النصاب، وقد توهم المقدسي في «الحاوي» أنه قيد احترازي فقال: لا تقبل شهادة النساء على الشهادة. انتهى. وهو غلط. انتهى ما في «البحر» بقدر الضرورة. وكذا لا يشترط أن يكون المشهود على شهادة رجلاً؛ لأن للمرأة أيضاً أن تشهد على شهادتها رجلين أو رجلاً وامرأتين، ويشترط أن يشهد على شهادة كل امرأة نصاب الشهادة، ذكره الزيلعي.
وقال البرجندي: واعلم أن هاهنا شرط آخر لم يذكره المصنف وهو أن الأصل لو كان حياً بقي وقت أداء الفرع جائز الشهادة، فلو خرس أو فسق أو عمى أو ارتد أو جن لم تجز شهادة الفرع. ذكره في «الخلاصة». انتهى.
[٢] قوله: لا تغاير فرعي هذا وذلك؛ أي لا يشترط تغاير فرعيها، بأن يكون لكل شاهد شاهدان متغايران، بل يكفي شاهدان على كل أصل، ولو قال: لا تغاير فرعيها كان أحسن.
The Summary of the End for the Support of the Care [Title]. [1] And testimony of a number [of witnesses] over each original, [with] no difference between its two branches. Obligatory as much as possible, and the witness is also a volunteer, so he is not burdened with hardship. And staying overnight away from one's family is a great hardship, so giving testimony upon his testimony is permitted to remove hardship from him and to revive people's rights. And the jurist Abu al-Layth and many of the scholars adopted this. And it is narrated from Muhammad that if the original witness is in a corner of the mosque and the secondary witness testifies upon his testimony in another corner of that mosque, his testimony is accepted. [1] His statement 'and testimony of a number... etc.': That is, a condition for it is the testimony of a complete number (nisab), which is two men or one man and two women over each original, because testimony is a right from among rights, so a complete number (nisab) of testimony is necessary. He said in al-Kubra: It is accepted in matters not invalidated by doubt if two men testify upon the testimony of two witnesses. End. So its apparent meaning is that this is a condition, so the testimony of women upon testimony is not accepted. And it is explicitly stated in al-Hawi al-Qudsi. But he said in al-Bahr: His statement 'if two men testify' occurred coincidentally, because it is permissible for a man and two women to testify upon it due to the completion of the nisab. And al-Maqdisi in al-Hawi mistakenly thought it was a restrictive condition and said: The testimony of women upon testimony is not accepted. End. And it is an error. End of what is in al-Bahr as needed. Likewise, it is not a condition that the person being testified upon regarding testimony be a man, because a woman may also have two men or one man and two women testify upon her testimony. And it is required that the nisab of testimony testify upon the testimony of each woman, as al-Zayla'i mentioned. And al-Barjandi said: Know that there is another condition here not mentioned by the author, which is that if the original is alive at the time the secondary performs, he must be capable of testifying. So if he becomes mute, or sinful, or blind, or an apostate, or insane, the secondary's testimony is not permissible. This is mentioned in al-Khulasa. End. [2] His statement 'no difference between its two branches this and that': That is, it is not required that its two branches be distinct, such that each witness has two distinct witnesses; rather, two witnesses over each original suffice. And if he had said 'no difference between its two branches', it would have been better.
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with explanation. The author states the condition for secondary testimony (shahadat al-far'): two witnesses (or one man and two women) must testify over each original witness. However, the same two secondary witnesses may testify over multiple originals; there is no need for distinct pairs for each original. The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to remove hardship (haraj) from the witness, especially if he must leave his home to testify. The passage also addresses a dispute regarding the acceptance of women's testimony in secondary testimony: the correct Hanafi position is that women's testimony is accepted if they complete the nisab (one man and two women). The commentator al-Barjandi adds a condition not found in the original text: the original witness must remain legally capable of testifying (i.e., not become mute, sinful, blind, apostate, or insane) at the time the secondary testifies. The key takeaway is that while the nisab requirement is strict, the identity of the secondary witnesses need not vary per original.

Key Terms
شهادة على الشهادة (shahāda ʿalā al-shahāda): Testimony given by a secondary witness based on the original testimony of another witness.
أصل (aṣl): The primary witness whose testimony is being transmitted.
فرع (farʿ): The secondary witness who testifies upon the original's testimony.
نصاب (niṣāb): The minimum number of witnesses required for validity: two men or one man and two women.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i: Number of Secondary Witnesses
Disputes the number of secondary witnesses needed for valid secondary testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that two secondary witnesses suffice for two original witnesses and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Two secondary witnesses testify to two separate rights, not one; thus the witness nisab is complete for each right.

This passage presents a classic dispute between the Hanafis and al-Shafi'i over secondary testimony (shahada 'ala al-shahada). The Shafi'i school requires four secondary witnesses, analogizing two secondary witnesses to two women in the original testimony. The Hanafi school, applying istihsan and analyzing the rights involved, holds that two secondary witnesses suffice. Let us examine the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequence of the difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling that the Hanafi school gives: two secondary witnesses are sufficient to testify over two original witnesses.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that al-Shafi'i requires four secondary witnesses: two for each original witness.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i qiyas: each pair of secondary witnesses stands in place of one original witness, analogous to two women standing in place of one man.
The Shafi'i argument is that just as two women cannot complete proof for a single right, two secondary witnesses cannot complete proof for two originals.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafi school refutes the analogy: the two secondary witnesses testify to two separate rights (the testimony of each original), so the nisab is complete for each right. This is different from two women testifying to a single right.
The Hanafi response is based on the principle that each original's testimony is a distinct obligation (haqq al-nas), and the two secondary witnesses discharge that obligation for each.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Note the practical difference: in a Hanafi court, two secondary witnesses are enough; in a Shafi'i court, four are required.
This affects court proceedings and the ease of establishing proof through secondary testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلافاً للشافعي له إذ عنده لا بد من أربعة شهداء، يشهد اثنان عن هذا وآخران عن ذلك، وعندنا يكفي اثنان يشهدان عن هذا ويشهدان عن ذلك.
[١] قوله: إذ عنده... إلخ؛ لأن كل شاهدين فرعين قائمان مقام شاهد أصل، فصار كالمرأتين، فإنهما لما قامتا مقام رجل واحد لم تتم حجة القضاء بشهادتهما، فكذا لا تتم حجة القضاء بهذين الشاهدين.
[٢] قوله: يكفي اثنان؛ لأن نقل شهادة الأصل من حقوق الناس، فإنه يجب على كل واحد من الأصلين أن يؤدي ما عليه إذا طلبه المدعى، فالشاهدان شهدا بحق ثم شهدا بحق آخر، فتقبل لكمال نصاب الشهادة، بخلاف شهادة المرأتين، فإن النصاب لم يوجد؛ لأنهما بمنزلة رجل واحد.
Contrary to al-Shafi'i, because according to him there must be four witnesses: two testify about this [original] and two others about that [original]. According to us, two suffice: they testify about this and testify about that. [1] His statement: 'because according to him... etc.': Because every two secondary witnesses stand in place of one original witness. Thus it becomes like the case of two women: when they stand in place of one man, the proof of judgment is not completed by their testimony. Similarly, the proof of judgment is not completed by these two witnesses [according to al-Shafi'i]. [2] His statement: 'two suffice': Because the transfer of the original's testimony is among the rights of people. Indeed, it is obligatory upon each of the two original witnesses to perform what is required of him when the claimant demands it. So the two witnesses testify regarding a right and then testify regarding another right, so it is accepted due to the completeness of the nisab of testimony, unlike the testimony of two women, for the nisab is not found because they are in the position of one man.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and al-Shafi'i regarding the number of secondary witnesses required for secondary testimony (shahada 'ala al-shahada). Al-Shafi'i requires four witnesses: two to testify over each of the two original witnesses, arguing that each pair of secondary witnesses replaces one original witness, just as two women replace one man. Therefore, just as two women alone do not constitute a complete proof, two secondary witnesses (one pair) cannot complete the proof for two originals. The Hanafi response, detailed in the commentary, distinguishes between the two situations. Transfer of testimony is a right of people (haqq al-nas), and it is obligatory for each original to give testimony when requested. The two secondary witnesses, by testifying over both originals, are testifying to two separate rights, and thus the nisab is complete for each transmission. In contrast, two women testifying to a single right are insufficient because the nisab for that single right is incomplete. The Hanafi position is that two secondary witnesses suffice as long as they witness the testimony of both originals.

Key Terms
شهادة على الشهادة (shahāda ʿalā al-shahāda): Testimony of a secondary witness based on the original testimony of another.
نصاب الشهادة (niṣāb al-shahāda): The minimum number of witnesses required for a valid testimony in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because secondary testimony is less important
B) Because each original's testimony is a separate right, and the two witnesses complete the nisab for each
C) Because the Shafi'i view is invalid
D) Because hardship demands leniency

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that two secondary witnesses suffice because they testify to two separate rights, completing the nisab for each.

Wording for Delegating Secondary Testimony
Details the precise wording the original witness must use for valid delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the required wording for delegating secondary testimony and explain why each part is necessary.

Core Idea
The original witness must use the exact wording 'اشهد على شهادتي أني أشهد بكذا' to validly delegate testimony; any variation risks nullity.

In Hanafi fiqh, when an original witness needs to delegate his testimony to a secondary witness, the delegation must be explicit and unambiguous. This lesson dissects the precise wording required, based on the principle that the secondary acts as an agent. Understanding this condition prevents invalid testimony that could undermine a legal claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components of the required phrasing
Break down the expression 'اشهد على شهادتي أني أشهد بكذا' into its core parts: the command 'ashhadu', the specification 'ala shahadati', and the content 'anni ashhadu bi-kadha'.
Each part serves a distinct legal purpose.
2
Understand the rationale for each restriction
The text gives reasons: 'ashhadu' is a command; 'ala shahadati' ties the testimony to the original's own testimony; 'anni ashhadu' clarifies that the secondary bears the original's testimony, not a separate statement.
Without 'ala', the phrase could be ambiguous; without 'anni', it might be an order to lie.
3
Compare with invalid alternatives
Consider phrases like 'ashhadu bi-shahadati' or 'ashhadu ala hadha' and explain why they fail: 'bi-' could mean 'by my testimony', implying falsehood; 'ala hadha' is too vague.
The text explicitly rules out these variants.
4
Apply the condition to a practical scenario
Imagine Zayd says to Amr: 'اشهد على شهادتي أني أشهد بأن عمراً لم يشهد.' Is that valid? The word 'anna' is acceptable, so yes. Then test a variant with 'bi' to see the invalidity.
Practice ensures retention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقول الأصل: اشهد على شهادتي أني أشهد بكذا.
[٣] قوله: ويقول الأصل... إلخ؛ شروع في بيان كيفية الإشهاد وأداء الفروع بعد الفراغ من بيان وجه مشروعيتها، وكمية الشهود للفروع، فقال: يقول الأصل: أشهد على شهادتي أني أشهد بكذا. ووجهه: أن الفرع كالنائب عن الأصل، والشهادة على الشهادة إنما تصير حجة بنقل شهادة الأصل إلى مجلس القضاء؛ فلذلك من التحميل والفرع وكيل من الأصل، فلا بد من التوكيل. وقيد بقوله: اشهد على شهادتي؛ لأنه لو لم يقل لم يمنعه أن يشهد على شهادته، وإن سمعها منه. وقيد بقوله: على شهادتي؛ لأنه لو قال: أشهد على هكذا، لم يجز له الشهادة؛ لأنه لفظ محتمل؛ لاحتمال أن يكون الإشهاد على نفس الحق المشهود به، فيكون أمراً بالكذب. وقيد بعلى؛ لأنه لو قال: بشهادتي، لم يجز له؛ لاحتمال أن يكون أمراً بأن يشهد مثل شهادته بالكذب.
And the original says: 'I bear witness upon my testimony that I bear witness to such-and-such.' [3] His statement: 'And the original says... etc.'; a commencement of explaining the manner of delegating testimony (ishhad) and the performance of the secondary witnesses after finishing the explanation of its legitimacy and the number of witnesses for the secondaries. So he said: The original says: 'I bear witness upon my testimony that I bear witness to such-and-such.' Its rationale: The secondary is like a deputy of the original, and testimony upon testimony becomes proof only by transferring the original's testimony to the court. So it is [a matter] of bearing (tahammul) and the secondary is an agent (wakil) from the original, so agency (tawkil) is necessary. He restricted it with his statement 'I bear witness upon my testimony' because if he did not say it, it would not prevent him from testifying upon his testimony, even if he heard it from him. And he restricted it with his statement 'upon my testimony' because if he said 'I bear witness to such-and-such', it would not be permissible for him to testify, because it is a probable expression; it is possible that the testifying is upon the same right being testified about, and that would be an order to lie. And he restricted it with 'upon' because if he said 'by my testimony', it would not be permissible for him; it is possible that it be an order to testify like his testimony in falsehood.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage explains the precise wording (lafz) that the original witness must use when delegating secondary testimony (ishhad). The original must say: 'I bear witness upon my testimony that I bear witness to such-and-such' (اشهد على شهادتي أني أشهد بكذا). The commentator explains that the secondary (far') acts as a deputy (na'ib) of the original, and testimony upon testimony becomes valid proof only by transmitting the original's testimony to the court. Thus the original must explicitly authorize the secondary using a clear expression of agency (tawkil). The wording must include three key restrictions: (1) using 'ashhadu' (I bear witness) to command the secondary; (2) saying 'ala shahadati' (upon my testimony) — without 'ala', the expression might be interpreted as a statement about the original's testimony, not a command; (3) using 'ala' instead of 'bi-' because 'bi-shahadati' could imply repeating the original's false testimony. These restrictions ensure no ambiguity that might lead to false testimony.

Key Terms
التحميل (al-taḥammul): The act of the original witness requiring the secondary to bear and transmit his testimony.
التوكيل (al-tawkīl): Agency; the original appoints the secondary as his agent to transmit testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) اشهد بشهادتي أني أشهد بكذا
B) اشهد على شهادتي أني أشهد بكذا
C) اشهد على هذا الحق
D) اشهد بشهادتي على هذا

Key Takeaway
The exact wording 'اشهد على شهادتي أني أشهد بكذا' is a condition for valid delegation of testimony in Hanafi law; any deviation invalidates the secondary testimony.

Number of Witnesses in Testimony Based on Testimony
Explains the dispute on number of witnesses for shahada 'ala al-shahada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the number of witnesses required for shahada 'ala al-shahada and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Two branch witnesses suffice because they testify for a right, not a deficiency.

When a witness cannot attend court, he may authorize another to testify on his behalf. A disputed question is how many branch witnesses are needed. The Hanafi school holds that two are enough, while the Shafi'i school requires four. This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
From the text: 'According to us, two witnesses suffice: they witness about this and about that.' State that two branch witnesses can testify about both original witnesses.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Understand the opposing view
The Shafi'i school requires four witnesses because each pair stands for one original. Their evidence is analogical (qiyas) to two women who together count as one witness.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The analogy with women is invalid because two women are deficient by nature (numerical deficiency), while two men are not. The branch witnesses are two men, so the number is complete.
The Hanafi reasoning is based on the right of people to have testimony transferred.
4
Determine the practical consequence
In Hanafi courts, two branch witnesses are accepted; in Shafi'i, four are needed. This affects the feasibility of using substitute testimony.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
