والفرع يقولُ : أشهد أن فلاناً أشهدني على شهادته بكذا، وقال : لي اشهد على شهادتي بذلك ) ، بعض المشايخ ابن طولوا وقالوا : يقولُ الأصلُ : أشهد بكذا وأنا أشهدك على شهادتي فاشهد على شهادتي وفيه خمس شينات، ويقول الفرع أشهد أن فلاناً شهد عندي بكذا ، فأشهدني على شهادته بكذا، وأمرني أن أشهد على شهادتِهِ بذلك ، وأنا أشهد على شهادته بذلك، وفيه ثماني شينات وقيَّد بالشهادة على الشهادة ؛ لأنَّ الشهادة بقضاء القاضي صحيحة، وإن لم يشهدها القاضي عليه. كذا في «البحر ) ، وغيره، وفي هذه الصفة في شهادة الأصل ثلاث شهادات، وفي شهادة الفرع خمس شهادات ، هذا في «الهداية»(۲)، وهو مختار شمس الأئمةِ الحَلْوَانِي . وعن الخصافِ له أنه يقول في الإشهاد : أشهد عندك بكذا، وأشهدك على شهادتي بذلك، فأنا أشهد على شهادته بذلك خفي شهادة الأصل أربع شهادات، وفي الفرع ثماني شهادات ، وبه أخذ أبو قاسم الصفار .
وعن الخصاف له أيضاً : إنّه يكفي أن يقول في الإشهاد : أشهدُ أنَّ فلاناً أقرَّ عندي لفلان بكذا، فاشهد على شهادتي أنَّ لفلان على فلان كذا، ويقول عند الأداء : أشهد أن فلانا شهد عندي أنَّ لفلان على فلان كذا، وأشهدني على شهادته، فالآن أشهد على شهادتِهِ أنَّ لفلان على فلان كذا، ففي الأصل ثلاث شهادات، وفي الفرع ست شهادات، وبهذا قال أبو نصر الله.
وذكر بعض أصحابنا أنه يقول في الأداء : أشهدُ أنَّ فلاناً شهد عندي بكذا ، وأشهدني على شهادته هذه ؛ لأشهد عليها عند الحاجة، فأنا أشهد على شهادته هذه ، وفيه سبع شهادات. كذا في «الفتاوى المنصورية». ذكره البرْجَندِي في «شرح النقاية». [ ۱ ] قوله : فلاناً فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ الفرع ينبغي أن يسمي الأصل عند القاضي، فإنَّ لفظ فلان كناية عن العلم ؛ وذلك لأنه إذا قال : لم أعرف اسمه، أو عرف ولم
Book of Testimony and Retraction. Disagreement in testimony. The original says: 'I bear witness based on my testimony that I witness such and such.' Contrary to al-Shafi'i, because according to him, four witnesses are necessary: two witness (one thing) and two others about that. According to us, two witnesses suffice: they witness about this and about that. (The original says: 'I bear witness based on my testimony that I witness such and such.' This. [1] His statement: 'because according to him...' because each two branch witnesses stand in place of one original witness, so it is like two women: since they stand in place of one man, the proof for judgment is not completed by their testimony, similarly the proof is not completed by these two witnesses. [2] His statement: 'two suffice' because transferring the testimony of the original is among the rights of people; for it is obligatory upon each of the two originals to perform what is upon them when the claimant requests it. So the two witnesses have testified for a right and then testify for another right, so it is accepted due to the completion of the number of testimony, unlike the testimony of two women, because the number is not found since they are in the position of one man. [3] His statement: 'And the original says...' is a beginning in explaining the manner of giving testimony and performance of branches after completing the explanation of its legitimacy and the number of witnesses for the branches. So he said: the original says: 'I bear witness...' etc. Its reasoning: the branch is like a deputy for the original, and testimony upon testimony becomes proof only by transferring the original's testimony to the court; because it is from loading and the branch is an agent of the original, so agency is necessary. He restricted with his statement: 'I bear witness based on my testimony' because if he did not say it, it would not prevent him from testifying on his testimony, though he heard it from him. He restricted with his statement: 'based on my testimony' because if he said: 'I bear witness on such', it would not be permissible for him to testify, because it is a word that is probable; due to the possibility that the giving of testimony is on the very right witnessed, so it would be an order of lying. He restricted with 'based on' because if he said: 'by my testimony', it would not be permissible for him, due to the possibility that it is an order that he testify like his testimony with lying. (1) But the relied-upon according to the Shafi'is is contrary to that; they do not require the two branch witnesses to be distinct from the two originals. Refer to al-Tanbih p.163, Mughni al-Muhtaj 4:455, Hashiyat Nihayat al-Muhtaj 10:276, etc. The branch says: 'I bear witness that so-and-so made me bear witness based on his testimony such-and-such, and said to me: bear witness based on my testimony to that.' Some mashayikh, Ibn Tulwa, said: the original says: 'I bear witness such-and-such and I make you bear witness based on my testimony, so bear witness based on my testimony.' In it there are five 'shin's. The branch says: 'I bear witness that so-and-so witnessed before me such-and-such, then he made me bear witness based on his testimony such-and-such, and ordered me that I bear witness on his testimony to that, and I bear witness on his testimony to that.' In it there are eight 'shin's. And it is restricted to testimony upon testimony because testimony based on a judge's ruling is valid even if the judge did not witness it upon him. Thus in al-Bahr and others. And in this description, in the testimony of the original there are three testimonies, and in the testimony of the branch there are five testimonies. This is in al-Hidayah (2), and it is the choice of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani. And from al-Khasaf, it is narrated that he says in giving testimony: 'I bear witness before you such-and-such, and I make you bear witness on my testimony to that, so I bear witness on his testimony to that.' So in the testimony of the original, four testimonies, and in the branch, eight testimonies, and Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar adopted it. And from al-Khasaf also: it is sufficient that he say in giving testimony: 'I bear witness that so-and-so acknowledged before me for so-and-so such-and-such, so bear witness on my testimony that for so-and-so against so-and-so such-and-such.' And at the time of performance he says: 'I bear witness that so-and-so witnessed before me that for so-and-so against so-and-so such-and-such, and made me bear witness on his testimony, and now I bear witness on his testimony that for so-and-so against so-and-so such-and-such.' So in the original there are three testimonies, and in the branch six testimonies, and Abu Nasr Allah said this. And some of our companions mentioned that he says in performance: 'I bear witness that so-and-so witnessed before me such-and-such, and made me bear witness on this testimony of his, so that I bear witness upon it when needed, so I bear witness on this testimony of his.' In it there are seven testimonies. Thus in al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyya. Al-Birjandi mentioned it in Sharh al-Nuqaya. [1] His statement: 'so-and-so' indicates that the branch should name the original before the judge, for the word 'so-and-so' is a metonymy for knowledge; because if he said: 'I do not know his name', or he knew but did not...
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the procedure for shahada 'ala al-shahada (testimony based on testimony) in the Hanafi school. It begins by contrasting the Hanafi position with that of al-Shafi'i regarding the number of witnesses. The Shafi'i school requires four witnesses: two to testify about the first original witness and two about the second. The Hanafi school holds that two witnesses suffice: the same two can testify about both originals. The reasoning is that transferring testimony is a right of the people, and each original is obliged to testify when requested. The two branch witnesses have already testified for one right and then testify for another, so their testimony is accepted as they complete the required number of witnesses. This is unlike two women, who together are considered one witness and thus do not complete the number. The passage then details the precise wording that the original witness must use when authorizing the branch. The original must say 'I bear witness based on my testimony that I witness such and such' to clearly indicate that the branch is acting as a deputy. If the wording is ambiguous, such as 'I bear witness on such' or 'by my testimony', it is invalid because it might imply that the branch is to testify directly about the right itself, which would be false. The branch then performs the testimony by saying 'I bear witness that so-and-so made me bear witness based on his testimony such-and-such, and said to me: bear witness based on my testimony to that.' The passage also records several formulations from different scholars, including the one from al-Hidayah preferred by Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, and others from al-Khasaf and later scholars, which vary in the number of 'testimonies' (shahadat) in the phrasing. Finally, it notes that the branch should name the original witness when testifying before the judge, using 'so-and-so' as a placeholder for a known name.

Key Terms
شهادة على الشهادة (shahāda ʿalā al-shahāda): Testimony based on another's testimony; a branch witness transmits the testimony of the original witness.
أصل (aṣl): The original witness who gives testimony and authorizes the branch to transmit it.
فرع (farʿ): The branch witness who receives and transmits the original's testimony to the court.
تحميل (taḥmīl): The act of the original loading the testimony onto the branch, i.e., making the branch a bearer of the testimony.
توكيل (tawkīl): Agency; the relationship between original and branch, where the branch is authorized to testify on behalf of the original.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many witnesses are sufficient for shahada 'ala al-shahada?
A) One
B) Two
C) Four
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
Two branch witnesses suffice for testimony based on testimony in the Hanafi school, as transferring testimony is a right of people and the witnesses are fully competent.

Module 9 · Lesson 140

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
10 min
Required Wording for Authorizing Branch Testimony
Explains the required wording for original and branch witnesses in testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conditions for valid wording in shahada 'ala al-shahada and identify the preferred formulation among the Hanafi versions.

Core Idea
Precise wording establishing agency is critical; ambiguous phrasing invalidates the testimony.

The validity of testimony based on testimony depends not only on the number of witnesses but on the exact wording used by the original and branch. Hanafi jurists meticulously crafted formulas to avoid any hint of perjury. This lesson explores the conditions and the various formulations recorded in the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for the original's wording
The original must explicitly say 'I bear witness based on my testimony' (اشهد على شهادتي) and avoid ambiguous phrases like 'I bear witness on such' or 'by my testimony'.
The text explains why each restriction is needed to prevent possible false testimony.
2
Identify the conditions for the branch's wording
The branch must respond with a phrase that includes 'I bear witness that so-and-so made me bear witness on his testimony...' and mention the original's authorization.
The branch acts as an agent and must clearly trace the testimony back to the original.
3
Understand why ambiguous phrases are invalid
Phrases like 'I bear witness on such' could imply direct testimony about the right itself, which would be false for the branch. Such ambiguity invalidates the testimony.
4
Review the multiple formulations
Several formulations exist, varying in the number of 'shahada' statements. The al-Hidayah formulation (3 in original, 5 in branch) is preferred by Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani.
Other formulations from al-Khasaf and others have 4/8, 3/6, or 7 in branch.
5
Recognize the significance of naming the original
The branch should name the original witness (using 'so-and-so' or actual name) when testifying, to avoid ambiguity about the source.
If the branch does not know the name, the testimony may be affected.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the procedure for shahada 'ala al-shahada (testimony based on testimony) in the Hanafi school. It begins by contrasting the Hanafi position with that of al-Shafi'i regarding the number of witnesses. The Shafi'i school requires four witnesses: two to testify about the first original witness and two about the second. The Hanafi school holds that two witnesses suffice: the same two can testify about both originals. The reasoning is that transferring testimony is a right of the people, and each original is obliged to testify when requested. The two branch witnesses have already testified for one right and then testify for another, so their testimony is accepted as they complete the required number of witnesses. This is unlike two women, who together are considered one witness and thus do not complete the number. The passage then details the precise wording that the original witness must use when authorizing the branch. The original must say 'I bear witness based on my testimony that I witness such and such' to clearly indicate that the branch is acting as a deputy. If the wording is ambiguous, such as 'I bear witness on such' or 'by my testimony', it is invalid because it might imply that the branch is to testify directly about the right itself, which would be false. The branch then performs the testimony by saying 'I bear witness that so-and-so made me bear witness based on his testimony such-and-such, and said to me: bear witness based on my testimony to that.' The passage also records several formulations from different scholars, including the one from al-Hidayah preferred by Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, and others from al-Khasaf and later scholars, which vary in the number of 'testimonies' (shahadat) in the phrasing. Finally, it notes that the branch should name the original witness when testifying before the judge, using 'so-and-so' as a placeholder for a known name.

Key Terms
شهادة على الشهادة (shahāda ʿalā al-shahāda): Testimony based on another's testimony; a branch witness transmits the testimony of the original witness.
أصل (aṣl): The original witness who gives testimony and authorizes the branch to transmit it.
فرع (farʿ): The branch witness who receives and transmits the original's testimony to the court.
تحميل (taḥmīl): The act of the original loading the testimony onto the branch, i.e., making the branch a bearer of the testimony.
توكيل (tawkīl): Agency; the relationship between original and branch, where the branch is authorized to testify on behalf of the original.

Wording Formulas and Ta'dil in Testimony upon Testimony
Explains wording formulas and ruling on ta'dil by branch witness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the acceptable wordings for shahada 'ala al-shahada according to Hanafi scholars and explain why a branch witness can adjust the original witness after transmission.

Core Idea
Multiple wordings for testimony upon testimony are valid; the branch witness, once his deputyship ends, can validly adjust the original.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explores the precise wording formulas required for shahada 'ala al-shahada (testimony upon testimony) and clarifies a related ruling: the branch witness's ability to adjust (ta'dil) the original witness after conveying the testimony. The text presents several intra-Hanafi opinions, demonstrating the school's flexibility in form while maintaining substance. It also addresses the concern of bias, affirming that a just branch witness is not suspected in such matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage deals with two distinct but related issues: the exact wording for original and branch witnesses in shahada 'ala al-shahada, and the permissibility of the branch witness (far') attesting to the integrity (ta'dil) of the original witness (asl) after conveying the testimony.
Understanding the separation of these issues is key.
2
Map the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on wording
List the various formulas from the scholars cited: al-Halwani (in al-Hidayah): original 3 shahadas, branch 5; al-Khassaf (first view): original 4, branch 8; al-Khassaf (second view): original 3, branch 6; some companions: original 7? Note the counts of the word 'shahada' in each formulation.
The number of 'shahada's varies, but all convey the same legal meaning.
3
Extract the preferred wording
The text states that the choice of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani (found in al-Hidayah) is the most prominent, but al-Sarakhsi's fatwa prefers the shortest wording (Qawm Abi Ja'far). The practical implication is that a judge may accept any clear wording.
Later Hanafi practice tends toward brevity.
4
Analyze the ruling on ta'dil
The branch witness, after conveying the testimony, becomes an outsider (ajnabi) — his deputyship ends. Therefore, his adjustment of the original's integrity is valid, just as one direct witness can adjust another.
This is analogous (qiyas) to one witness adjusting another.
5
Evaluate the objections and responses
Some argue the branch witness is suspect (muttahim) because he wants his own testimony executed. The response: a just person ('adl) is not suspected in such matters, just as a witness is not suspected in his own direct testimony.
The principle: 'The just is not doubted.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرع يقولُ: أشهد أن فلاناً أشهدني على شهادته بكذا، وقال: لي اشهد على شهادتي بذلك ، بعض المشايخ ابن طولوا وقالوا: يقولُ الأصلُ: أشهد بكذا وأنا أشهدك على شهادتي فاشهد على شهادتي وفيه خمس شينات، ويقول الفرع أشهد أن فلاناً شهد عندي بكذا، فأشهدني على شهادته بكذا، وأمرني أن أشهد على شهادتِهِ بذلك، وأنا أشهد على شهادته بذلك، وفيه ثماني شينات وقيَّد بالشهادة على الشهادة؛ لأنَّ الشهادة بقضاء القاضي صحيحة، وإن لم يشهدها القاضي عليه. كذا في «البحر»، وغيره، وفي هذه الصفة في شهادة الأصل ثلاث شهادات، وفي شهادة الفرع خمس شهادات، هذا في «الهداية»، وهو مختار شمس الأئمةِ الحَلْوَانِي. وعن الخصافِ له أنه يقول في الإشهاد: أشهد عندك بكذا، وأشهدك على شهادتي بذلك، فأنا أشهد على شهادته بذلك خفي شهادة الأصل أربع شهادات، وفي الفرع ثماني شهادات، وبه أخذ أبو قاسم الصفار. وعن الخصاف له أيضاً: إنّه يكفي أن يقول في الإشهاد: أشهدُ أنَّ فلاناً أقرَّ عندي لفلان بكذا، فاشهد على شهادتي أنَّ لفلان على فلان كذا، ويقول عند الأداء: أشهد أن فلانا شهد عندي أنَّ لفلان على فلان كذا، وأشهدني على شهادته، فالآن أشهد على شهادتِهِ أنَّ لفلان على فلان كذا، ففي الأصل ثلاث شهادات، وفي الفرع ست شهادات، وبهذا قال أبو نصر الله. وذكر بعض أصحابنا أنه يقول في الأداء: أشهدُ أنَّ فلاناً شهد عندي بكذا، وأشهدني على شهادته هذه؛ لأشهد عليها عند الحاجة، فأنا أشهد على شهادته هذه، وفيه سبع شهادات. كذا في «الفتاوى المنصورية». ذكره البرْجَندِي في «شرح النقاية». قوله: فلاناً فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ الفرع ينبغي أن يسمي الأصل عند القاضي، فإنَّ لفظ فلان كناية عن العلم؛ وذلك لأنه إذا قال: لم أعرف اسمه، أو عرف ولم يسمه، للقاضي أن لا يقبل شهادته. صرح في «الخزانة»، وغيرها. قوله: السِّرَخْسِيُّ؛ أي المنسوب إلى السرخس .. بفتح السين والراء المهملتين - بلدة عظيمة بخراسان. كذا في «القاموس». والمراد به شمس الأئمة محمد بن أحمد بن أبي سهل له، وهو مشهور في الفقه والدراية غاية الاشتهار، أخذ عن شمس الأئمة الحلواني وغيره من الأعلام الأخيار، وله شرح «المبسوط» وشرح «السير الكبير وكتاب في أصول الفقه، وإن شئت تفصيل حالاته، فارجع إلى أعلام الأخيار. قوله: صح؛ أي تعديله، فإنَّ الفرع نائب ناقل عبارة الأصل إلى مجلس القاضي، فبالنقل ينتهي علم النيابة، فيصيرُ أجنبياً، فيصح تعديله، قال في «المنح»: المراد أنَّ الفروع يعرفون بالعدالة عند القاضي فعدلُّوا الأصول، وإن لم يعرفهم بها فلا بد من تعديلهم، وتعديل أصولهم. انتهى. قوله: كأحد الشاهدين؛ أي كتعديل أحد الشاهدين للآخر، فإنّه من أهل التزكية، ومنهم من قال: لا يصح تعديله؛ لأنه يريد تنفيذ شهادة نفسه بهذا التعديل، فكان منهماً. والجواب أنَّ العدل لا يتّهمُ بمثله، كما لا يتّهمُ في شهادة نفسه، فإنّه يحتمل أن
And the branch witness says: 'I bear witness that so-and-so made me bear witness to his testimony such-and-such, and said: Bear witness to my testimony to that.' Some mashayikh (like Ibn Tulun?) said: The original witness says: 'I bear witness to such-and-such, and I make you a witness over my testimony, so bear witness to my testimony'—and in it there are five 'shahad's. And the branch witness says: 'I bear witness that so-and-so testified before me to such-and-such, so he made me bear witness to his testimony to such-and-such, and ordered me to bear witness to his testimony to that, and I bear witness to his testimony to that'—and in it there are eight 'shahad's. And it is restricted to testimony upon testimony because testimony regarding the judge's ruling is valid even if the judge did not hear it. Thus in 'al-Bahr' and others. In this description, the original's testimony contains three testimonies, and the branch's contains five. This is in 'al-Hidayah', and it is the choice of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani. And from al-Khassaf: he said concerning the initial witnessing: 'I bear witness before you to such-and-such, and I make you a witness over my testimony to that, so I bear witness to his testimony to that.' So the original's testimony contains four testimonies, and the branch's contains eight. Abu Qasim al-Saffar adopted this. And from al-Khassaf also: it is sufficient that he says in the initial witnessing: 'I bear witness that so-and-so acknowledged before me to so-and-so such-and-such, so bear witness to my testimony that so-and-so has a right against so-and-so such-and-such.' And at the time of performance he says: 'I bear witness that so-and-so testified before me that so-and-so has a right against so-and-so such-and-such, and he made me bear witness to his testimony, so now I bear witness to his testimony that so-and-so has a right against so-and-so such-and-such.' So the original contains three testimonies, and the branch contains six. Abu Nasr Allah said this. And some of our companions mentioned that he says at the time of performance: 'I bear witness that so-and-so testified before me to such-and-such, and he made me bear witness to this his testimony, so that I may bear witness upon it when needed, so I bear witness to this his testimony.' In it are seven testimonies. Thus in 'al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyya'. Al-Barjandi mentioned it in 'Sharh al-Nuqaya'. His statement: 'so-and-so' indicates that the branch witness should name the original witness before the judge, for the word 'so-and-so' is a substitute for knowledge. That is because if he says, 'I do not know his name,' or knows but does not name him, the judge may reject his testimony. This is stated clearly in 'al-Khazana' and others. His statement: 'al-Sarakhsi' means the one attributed to Sarakhs... a great city in Khorasan. Thus in 'al-Qamus'. What is meant is Shams al-A'imma Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Abi Sahl, famous in fiqh and knowledge, studied under Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani and other notable scholars, and he wrote a commentary on 'al-Mabsut' and a commentary on 'al-Siyar al-Kabir' and a book on usul al-fiqh. If you wish details of his life, refer to 'A'lam al-Akhyar'. His statement: 'valid' means his adjustment, for the branch witness is a deputy conveying the words of the original to the court, so by conveying, the deputyship ends, he becomes a stranger, so his adjustment is valid. He said in 'al-Minhaj': The meaning is that the branch witnesses are known for their integrity before the judge, so they adjust the original witnesses. If the judge does not know them, they must be adjusted, and their origins must be adjusted. End. His statement: 'like one of the two witnesses' means like the adjustment of one witness for the other, for he is among those qualified for tazkiya. Some say: his adjustment is not valid because he wants to execute his own testimony by this adjustment, so he is suspect. The answer: the just person is not suspected in such a matter, just as he is not suspected in his own testimony, because it is possible that...
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage deals with the topic of shahada 'ala al-shahada (testimony upon testimony), a specific form of evidence where the original witness (al-asl) testifies before the branch witness (al-far'), who then transmits that testimony to the court. The text begins by presenting various opinions of Hanafi scholars regarding the exact wording that should be used for the original and branch testimonies. Some scholars emphasize the number of times the word 'shahada' appears, with different counts (3/5, 4/8, 3/6, 7) reflecting variations in the phrasing. The preferred view according to Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani (as in al-Hidayah) is the first formulation with three testimonies from the original and five from the branch. The passage then discusses that the branch witness should name the original witness before the judge; failure to do so may lead to rejection. It then introduces a sub-issue: if the branch witness (after conveying the testimony) adjusts the original witness by attesting to their integrity (tazkiya), this adjustment is valid because the branch witness, once the testimony is conveyed, becomes a third party (ajnabi) and is no longer acting as a deputy. This is likened to one of two direct witnesses adjusting the other, which is accepted. The objection that the branch witness might be biased is refuted by the principle that a just person is not suspected in like matters. The passage ends abruptly, but the main rulings are clear: the wording of shahada 'ala al-shahada is not rigid, and the branch witness can validly adjust the original after performing his role.

Key Terms
شهادة على الشهادة (shahāda ʿalā al-shahāda): Testimony upon testimony: a witness (farʿ) testifies to what another witness (aṣl) had previously testified, effectively transmitting the aṣl's testimony to the court.
أصل (aṣl): The original witness who directly observed the event and gives his testimony to the branch witness.
فرع (farʿ): The branch witness who receives the testimony from the original witness and transmits it to the judge.
تعديل (taʿdīl): Adjusting: attesting to the integrity (ʿadāla) of a witness, making him acceptable to the court.
مختار شمس الأئمة الحلواني (mukhtār Shams al-A'imma al-Ḥalwānī): The preferred view of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, which is the formula in al-Hidayah (3/5).

A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
Multiple wordings for shahada 'ala al-shahada are valid; the branch witness can validly adjust the original after transmission because his deputyship ends.

Branch Witness Modifying Original: Validity and Analogy
Ruling and proof that a branch witness's modification of the original is valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a branch witness's modification of the original is valid, using the analogy to one witness modifying another.

Core Idea
A branch witness, after transmitting testimony, becomes an outsider; his modification of the original is valid by analogy to one witness modifying another.

The branch witness (al-far') originally transmits testimony from the original witness (al-asl) to the court. After transmission, his role as agent ends. Now, if he wishes to testify to the original's probity (tazkiyah), does his statement count? This lesson unpacks the ruling and the reasoning behind it, addressing a common objection about bias.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What specific act is being ruled upon? What is the hukm (level of ruling) according to the text?
The act is the branch witness's modification (ta'dil) of the original witness. The ruling is 'sahih' (valid).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
What proof does the author cite for this ruling? Identify the type of evidence.
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to one witness modifying another, as indicated by the phrase 'كأحد الشاهدين الآخر'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal link)
How exactly does the analogy apply? What is the common effective cause (illa)?
After transmission, the branch becomes a stranger (ajnabi) to the case, just as one witness is a stranger to the other's testimony. Thus, his modification is like that of an outsider.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Does any scholar or school disagree? What is their objection?
Some argue that modification is invalid because the branch wants to enforce his own testimony (tuhma). The text provides a rebuttal.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preferred view)
Which position is relied upon for fatwa and why?
The Hanafi position, upheld by Imam al-Sarakhsi, is that the modification is valid because a just person is not accused of bias.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن عدل الفرعُ أصله صح كأحد الشاهدين الآخر، وإن سكت عنه نظر في حاله والأحسنُ الأقصر قول أبي جعفر أن يقول الأصلُ : اشهد على شهادتي بكذا ، أو يقول الفرعُ : أشهد على شهادةِ فلان بكذا من غير احتياج إلى ذكر زيادة ، وعليه فتوى الإمام السَّرَخْسِي. [٢] قوله : صح؛ أي تعديله، فإنَّ الفرع نائب ناقل عبارة الأصل إلى مجلس القاضي، فبالنقل ينتهي علم النيابة، فيصيرُ أجنبياً ، فيصح تعديله، قال في «المنح» : المراد أنَّ الفرع يعرف بالعدالة عند القاضي فعدلُّوا الأصول، وإن لم يعرفهم بها فلا بد من تعديلهم، وتعديل أصولهم. [٣] قوله : كأحد الشاهدين ؛ أي كتعديل أحد الشاهدين للآخر، فإنّه من أهل التزكية، ومنهم من قال : لا يصح تعديله ؛ لأنه يريد تنفيذ شهادة نفسه بهذا التعديل ، فكان منهماً. والجواب أنَّ العدل لا يتّهمُ بمثله، كما لا يتّهمُ في شهادة نفسه، فإنّه يحتمل أن يقال : إنما شهد فيما شهد ؛ ليصير مقبول القول فيما بين الناس عند تنفيذ القاضي قوله على موجب ما شهد، وإن لم يكن له شهادة فيه في الواقع، ولا وجه للتهمة، فإنّ قولَه مقبول في حق نفسه ، وإن ردَّت شهادة صاحبه حتى إذا انضم إلـيـه غـيـره مـن العـدول حكم القاضي بشهادتهما فلا تهمة فيه.
If the branch modifies the original, it is valid, like one of the two witnesses modifying the other. If he remains silent about him, his condition is examined. The best and shortest is the statement of Abu Ja'far that the original says: 'I bear witness on my testimony of such-and-such,' or the branch says: 'I bear witness to the testimony of so-and-so of such-and-such' without need to mention an increase. And upon this is the fatwa of Imam al-Sarakhsi. [2] He said: 'valid' means his modification. For the branch is a deputy, transmitting the words of the original to the court; by the transmission the agency ends, so he becomes a stranger, thus his modification is valid. He said in al-Minah: The intended meaning is that the branch is known for probity before the judge, so they modified the originals. If they are not known to him, there must be their modification and the modification of their originals. [3] He said: 'like one of the two witnesses' i.e. like the modification of one witness by the other, for he is among those qualified for character endorsement. Some say his modification is not valid because he wants to execute his own testimony by this modification, so he is accused. The answer is that the just person is not accused of such, just as he is not accused in his own testimony. For it is possible that he testified in what he testified to become acceptable in speech among people upon the judge's enforcement of his statement according to what he testified, even if he had no testimony in it in reality. And there is no ground for accusation. Indeed, his statement is accepted concerning his own right, even if the testimony of his companion is rejected, until when another just person joins him, the judge judges by their testimony. So there is no accusation.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its analogy-based proof concerning a branch witness (al-far') modifying the original (al-asl). The ruling states that if the branch witness, who originally transmitted testimony on behalf of the original, later modifies (tazkiyah) the original, it is valid (sahih). The basis is analogy with one witness modifying the other in a direct testimony. The preferred method (al-ahsan al-aqsar) for such transmission is either the original saying 'I bear witness on my testimony of such-and-such' or the branch saying 'I bear witness to the testimony of so-and-so of such-and-such' without adding extra details. The fatwa of Imam al-Sarakhsi supports this. The commentary explains that the branch becomes a stranger (ajnabi) after transmitting, so his modification is valid. It also addresses an objection: some argue that modification is invalid because the branch wants to enforce his own testimony and is therefore biased. The response: a just person is not accused of such bias, just as his own testimony is accepted. The hypothetical suspicion that he only testified to be believed is unfounded because his statement is accepted in his own right even if his co-witness is not. Thus, the analogy holds.

Key Terms
الفرع (al-far'): The branch witness who transmits testimony from the original witness to the court; a secondary witness.
الأصل (al-asl): The original witness who directly witnessed the event and from whom the branch transmits testimony.
تعديل (ta'dil): Modification; a testimony that a witness is 'adl (just and upright).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Why is the branch's modification of the original considered valid in the Hanafi view?
A) Because the branch is always considered just.
B) Because the branch becomes a stranger after transmitting the testimony, thus his modification is like that of an outsider.
C) Because the original's testimony is not needed.
D) Because the branch is the same as the original.

Key Takeaway
A branch witness's modification of the original is valid because after transmission he is an outsider, and a just person is not subject to tuhma.

Shahadat — Testimony

Dispute: Branch Witness Must Know Original's Probity?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on branch witness's knowledge of original's probity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether a branch witness must know the original's probity, explain the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Probity must be established before the judge, not necessarily known to the branch.

This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. The question is whether a branch witness (far') must personally know the probity ('adalah) of the original witness for his testimony to be accepted. The ruling affects how testimonial chains are validated in court.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
The passage presents a case where two branch witnesses testify based on information from two originals. The key issue: the branch does not know the original's probity.
The original denies giving testimony, which invalidates the branch's testimony outright, but the dispute focuses on probity knowledge.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Yusuf accepts the testimony if probity is established before the judge; Muhammad rejects unless the branch knows probity.
3
Analyze the evidence
Muhammad argues that no testimony is valid without probity. Abu Yusuf argues the branch is merely a transmitter; probity is a condition for the judge to verify.
The branch's role is to transmit, not to attest to probity.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The later scholars adopt Abu Yusuf's view, as indicated by 'قلنا' (We said) in the text.
This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, the judge can accept the branch's testimony after independently verifying the original's probity.
The branch does not need to know probity personally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان أنكر الأصل شهادته بطل شهادة فرعه، ولو شهدا عن اثنين على عزة بنت عزّ المضري، وقالا : أخبرانا بمعرفتها، وجاء المدعي بامرأة لم يدريا بأنها هي أم لا فإن ثبت عدالته تقبل شهادة فرعِهِ هذا عند أبي يوسف ، وعند محمد : لا تقبل إذ لا شهادة إلا بالعدالة ، فإذا لم يَعْرِفِ الفرع عدالة الأصل ، لم تقبل شهادته فلا يقبل شهادة الفرع . قلنا: لا يشترط معرفة الفرع عدالة الأصل، بل يشترط أن يثبت ذلك عند القاضي ، فإن ثبت عنده يقبله وإلا لا.
And if the original denies his testimony, the testimony of his branch is invalid. And if two (branches) testify on behalf of two (originals) concerning 'Azza bint 'Izz al-Mudari, and they say: 'They informed us of her identity,' and the plaintiff brings a woman, and they do not know if she is the same or not, then if (the originals') probity is established, the testimony of this branch is accepted according to Abu Yusuf. And according to Muhammad, it is not accepted, for there is no testimony except with probity, so if the branch does not know the probity of the original, his testimony is not accepted. We said: It is not a condition that the branch know the probity of the original; rather it is a condition that this be established before the judge, for if it is established before him, he accepts it, otherwise not.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage contains an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the acceptance of a branch's testimony when the branch does not personally know the probity of the original. The scenario: two branch witnesses testify that two originals informed them of a woman's identity (e.g., that she is a specific person). The plaintiff brings a woman, and the branches do not know if she is the same one. If the probity of the originals is established before the judge, Abu Yusuf says the branch's testimony is accepted because the branch's duty is merely to transmit; the judge will investigate probity. Muhammad says it is not accepted because the branch must himself know the original's probity; otherwise, the testimony lacks the condition of 'adalah. The passage then gives the Hanafi response (presumably the preferred or majority view): it is not required for the branch to know the probity; it is sufficient that probity be established before the judge. This aligns with Abu Yusuf's view. The passage also mentions the effect of the original's denial: if the original denies having given testimony, the branch's testimony becomes invalid.

Key Terms
عدالة ('adalah): Probity; the quality of being upright and just, a condition for the acceptance of testimony.
فرع (far'): Branch witness; one who transmits testimony from the original.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi position in this passage, when is a branch's testimony accepted if the branch does not know the original's probity?
A) Never, because the branch must know the probity.
B) Only if the original is present to confirm.
C) If the probity of the original is established before the judge.
D) Only if the branch swears an oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, branch witnesses do not need to personally know the original's probity; it suffices that probity is established before the judge.

Subsidiary Testimony: Denial by Original Witness
Ruling on acceptance of subsidiary testimony when original denies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of the original witness's denial on the subsidiary's testimony and distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the subsidiary's knowledge of the original's integrity.

Core Idea
If the original witness denies appointing the subsidiary, the subsidiary's testimony is void—denial before judgment breaks the chain.

This passage examines a critical condition for subsidiary testimony: the original's appointment (tahmil). The commentator clarifies that if the original denies having made the subsidiary a witness, the subsidiary's testimony becomes void. An intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is also resolved: the subsidiary need not personally know the original's integrity; the judge investigates it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The text rules that if the original witness denies his testimony, the subsidiary's testimony becomes void. The key condition is that this denial occurs before the judge's judgment.
Denial after judgment does not nullify the already passed judgment.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that the subsidiary need only transmit the original's testimony; the judge checks the original's integrity. Muhammad requires the subsidiary to personally know the original's integrity. Contrast the two positions.
The commentator sides with Abu Yusuf.
3
Trace the evidence for each position
Abu Yusuf argues that the subsidiary's duty is transmission (naql), not modification (ta'dil). Muhammad argues that testimony is only valid with known integrity.
The Hanafi view is that the judge can ascertain integrity independently.
4
Apply the mufta bihi view
The adopted Hanafi position is that the subsidiary need not know the original's integrity; it suffices that the judge establishes it.
This is based on the commentator's 'qulna' statement.
5
Note the temporal qualification
The text specifies that denial nullifies testimony only if it occurs before the judgment. If the original denies after the judge has ruled, the judgment remains valid.
This is clarified in the parenthetical remark from al-Khulasa.

(المراد بالإنكار هو الإنكار قبل الحكم، فإنه لو أنكر بعد الحكم لم تبطل، فإن المراد ببطلان الشهادة عدم قبولها، والحكمُ الواقع قبل الإنكار لا يبطل. كما صرحوا به.)
And if the original witness denies his testimony, the testimony of his subsidiary becomes void. And if two testify about two persons regarding 'Izza bint 'Izz al-Mudari and say: 'They informed us of her acquaintance,' and the plaintiff brings a woman and they do not know whether she is that woman or not, then if her integrity is established, the testimony of this subsidiary is accepted according to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad: it is not accepted, for testimony is only with integrity; so if the subsidiary does not know the integrity of the original, his testimony is not accepted, so the subsidiary's testimony is not accepted. We say: It is not a condition for the subsidiary to know the integrity of the original; rather it is a condition that this be established before the judge; if it is established with him, he accepts it, otherwise not. And if the original denies his testimony, the testimony of his subsidiary becomes void. And if they testify about two persons regarding 'Izza bint 'Izz al-Mudari and say: 'They informed us of her acquaintance,' and the plaintiff brings a woman and they do not know whether she is that woman or not. It is said: He only testified in what he testified to become acceptable of speech among people when the judge implements his statement according to what he testified, even if he has no testimony in it in reality, and there is no basis for suspicion; for his statement is accepted regarding himself, even if the testimony of his companion is rejected; so when someone else from the upright joins him, the judge judges by their testimony, so there is no suspicion in it. [1] His statement: 'This according to Abu Yusuf'; its reasoning: The obligation on the subsidiary is transmission, not modification; because modification may be hidden from him. And when the subsidiary transmits the testimony of the original, the judge ascertains the integrity, as if the original himself attended and testified, then the judge ascertains his condition. [2] His statement: 'We say...'; its summary: It is not a condition in transmission that the judge know the integrity of the original; rather it is a condition in acceptance of testimony that integrity be established before the judge. If it is established with him, he accepts it; otherwise not. [3] His statement: 'And if he denies...'; the scenario of the issue: If the original says regarding the subsidiary: 'I do not know him and did not make him a witness on my testimony,' then dies or disappears, and then that subsidiary comes and testifies before the judge, the judge does not accept his testimony; because the burden of proof (tahmil) is a condition, and it is not established due to conflict between the report of the original and the subsidiary's report; because it is probable that the original is truthful, so the burden is not established with probability. And it is qualified by denial; because if he was asked and remained silent, the appointing (ishhad) is not invalidated. As in 'al-Manh' quoting from 'al-Khulasa'. (What is meant by denial is denial before the judgment; if he denies after the judgment, it does not nullify; for what is meant by nullification of testimony is its non-acceptance, and a judgment that occurred before the denial is not nullified, as they have clarified.)
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on rulings regarding shahada 'ala al-shahada (subsidiary testimony). The commentator explains three key points. First, if the original witness denies having appointed the subsidiary, the subsidiary's testimony is void. Second, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding whether the subsidiary must personally know the original's 'adala (integrity). Abu Yusuf says the subsidiary need only transmit; the judge will investigate the original's 'adala. Muhammad requires the subsidiary to know it because testimony without known 'adala is invalid. The adopted Hanafi position (as stated by the commentator) is that knowledge of 'adala is not a condition for the subsidiary; it suffices that the judge establishes it. Third, the commentators clarify that the denial that nullifies testimony is only before judgment. If the original denies after the judge has ruled, the judgment remains valid. The discussion also addresses the scenario where two witnesses testify on the identity of a defendant; they need not personally recognize the person brought—their testimony is about the description, not the specific individual. The commentator rebuts a potential suspicion of conflict of interest, emphasizing that the subsidiary's testimony is accepted only when accompanied by other upright witnesses.

Key Terms
الفرع (al-far'): The subsidiary witness who testifies on the basis of another's testimony (shahada 'ala al-shahada).
الأصل (al-asl): The original witness who directly observed the event and appointed the subsidiary to testify.
التحميل (al-tahmil): The act of the original witness burdening the subsidiary with the duty to transmit his testimony; a condition for subsidiary testimony.
الإنكار (al-inkār): Denial by the original witness that he appointed the subsidiary or gave the testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The subsidiary's testimony is void.
B) The subsidiary's testimony is still accepted if he is upright.
C) The judge must investigate both parties.
D) The judgment already passed remains nullified.

Key Takeaway
Denial by the original witness before judgment voids the subsidiary's testimony, but the subsidiary need not know the original's integrity—the judge verifies it.

Identification of the Defendant in Testimony: The Need for Confirming Witnesses
Ruling on needing witnesses to identify the defendant brought to court

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on identifying a defendant when witnesses do not know him/her personally, apply the rule to judicial letters and property-boundary analogies, and distinguish the intra-Hanafi difference on sufficiency of lineage identification.

Core Idea
Witnesses need not know the defendant personally; the plaintiff must bring two confirming witnesses to match description to person.

When witnesses testify based on a description (e.g., 'I testify against 'Izza bint 'Izz al-Mudari'), they may not recognize the defendant in court. The Hanafi school provides a practical solution: the plaintiff must produce two additional witnesses to confirm the identity. This lesson explores that ruling, the conditions for identification, and the intra-madhhab difference regarding the need for grandfather or alternative identifiers like street or clan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core problem
Understand that the issue arises when witnesses give testimony based on a description but do not personally know the defendant. The question is whether such testimony can be executed.
The witnesses may have never seen the defendant before.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The plaintiff is asked to bring two witnesses who testify that the person brought before the court is indeed the one described in the original testimony. This is a wajib (obligatory) step.
The phrase 'هات شاهدين أنها عزَّة' illustrates this.
3
Understand the condition of complete lineage
For the original testimony to be acceptable, the description must sufficiently identify the defendant. If the grandfather's name is omitted, the identification must be completed by referring to a specific street (sikka) or clan (fakhdh).
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Yusuf holds that mentioning the grandfather is not a condition at all, so substitutes like street or clan are also unnecessary. Under his view, a simple name like 'Izza bint Izz al-Mudari' is sufficient.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) view is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
Extend to judicial letters and analogies
The same rule applies when a judge sends a written testimony (kitab hukmi) to another judge. The plaintiff must bring witnesses to confirm the identity of the described person. The commentator also draws an analogy to property boundaries: if boundaries are mentioned without personal knowledge, confirming witnesses are needed.
This shows the principle is consistent across different types of cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وكذا الكتاب الحكمي) : أي إذا جاء كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي، ولا يعرف الشهود المشهود عليه، قيل للمدعي: هات شاهدين أن هذا هو المشهود عليه. It was said to him: 'Bring two witnesses that she is 'Izza.' And similarly the judicial letter: i.e., when a judge's letter comes to another judge, and the witnesses do not know the person against whom testimony is given, it is said to the plaintiff: 'Bring two witnesses that this is the one against whom testimony is given.' Know that the purpose of this issue is that it is not a condition that the witnesses know the person against whom they testify (the defendant); rather it is said to the plaintiff: 'Bring two witnesses who testify that the one you have brought is the one against whom testimony is given.' And it is not intended that if they testify about so-and-so daughter of so-and-so of Mudar, the lineage is complete and the testimony is accepted; because if he does not mention the grandfather, it is necessary to attribute to the small alley (sikka) or to the clan (fakhdh) — i.e., the specific tribe — so that the lineage is complete, and the testimony is accepted according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf; for mentioning the grandfather is not a condition with him, so what takes its place of mentioning the alley or clan is not required. (And similarly the judicial letter): i.e., when the letter of a judge comes to another judge, and the witnesses do not know the person against whom testimony is given, it is said to the plaintiff: 'Bring two witnesses that this is the one against whom testimony is given.' [1] His statement: 'It was said to him...' because identification by lineage is realized by their testimony, and the plaintiff claims that that lineage applies to the present woman, and it is possible that it applies to someone else, so it is necessary to establish that lineage for the present woman. An analogy: if they testify about a sale of a limited property, mentioning its boundaries without knowing its very object, and they testify against the opponent, then it is necessary that two others testify that the limited property with those boundaries is in the possession of the defendant, so the judgment is valid. Similarly if the defendant states that the boundaries mentioned in the testimony are the boundaries of what is in his hand, then two other witnesses are needed to testify that the mentioned boundaries are the boundaries of what is in his hand. [2] His statement: 'So-and-so daughter of so-and-so'; he presented them as indefinite (without 'alif lam') based on what is in 'al-Misbah': 'fulan' and 'fulanah' without 'alif lam' are epithets for humans, and with 'alif lam' are epithets for animals; it is said: 'I rode the fulan,' 'I brought the fulanah.' End. [3] His statement: 'i.e., when ...' means that the judge wrote in his letter: 'Two upright witnesses testified that so-and-so son of so-and-so the such-and-such has upon so-and-so daughter of so-and-so the such-and-such such-and-such, so you judge against them in my presence.'
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage addresses a practical issue in court: when witnesses testify based on a description (e.g., 'I testify against 'Izza bint 'Izz al-Mudari') but do not visually recognize the defendant brought before the court. The ruling is that this does not invalidate their testimony; rather, the plaintiff must produce two additional witnesses to confirm that the person present is indeed the one described. This requirement ensures that the identity is legally established. The commentator also discusses the sufficiency of lineage identification. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if the grandfather is not mentioned, the testimony is still accepted if the defendant is identified by a specific street (sikka) or clan (fakhdh). Abu Yusuf, however, does not require the grandfather's mention and thus does not require substitute identifiers. The passage then extends the same principle to judicial letters: when a judge sends a written testimony to another judge, and the witnesses did not personally know the defendant, the plaintiff must bring witnesses to match the person to the description. The commentator provides an analogy from property boundaries to illustrate the principle. Finally, a grammatical note clarifies that 'fulan' without 'alif lam' is used for humans, with 'alif lam' for animals.

Key Terms
السكة الصغيرة (al-sikka al-saghira): The small street or lane used as a means of identifying a person's locality.
الفخذ (al-fakhdh): A clan or subtribe; a division of a tribe used in personal identification.
الكتاب الحكمي (al-kitab al-hukmi): A judicial letter from one judge to another containing testimony that needs execution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The plaintiff must bring two witnesses that the woman present is that same 'Izza.
B) The witnesses must be asked to identify her from a lineup.
C) The testimony is rejected for lack of certainty.
D) The judge may accept the testimony without further proof.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when witnesses do not know the defendant personally, the plaintiff must provide two confirming witnesses to match the description to the person, and lineage must be adequately identified (either with grandfather or a specific street/clan per Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).

Shahadat — Testimony

Specifying the Sub-Tribe in Witness Testimony
Ruling with rational proof on testimony identification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition that witness testimony for Arabs requires specifying the sub-tribe (fakhdh) and distinguish the alternative for non-Arabs using profession.

Core Idea
A general tribal name fails to identify; the sub-tribe must be specified for testimony to be valid.

This lesson examines a ruling from Al-Hidayah on the conditions for accepting witness testimony, particularly when identifying a person by lineage. The core principle is that a general attribution like 'al-Mudariyyah' does not uniquely identify a person, so the sub-tribe (fakhdh) must be mentioned. The commentary explains the levels of Arab lineage and draws analogies to geographical descriptions to illustrate why specificity is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the opening sentence: 'If the two witnesses say: al-Mudariyyah, it is not permissible until they attribute her to her sub-tribe.' The ruling is a condition: the testimony is not accepted unless the sub-tribe is specified.
This applies to 'testimony on testimony' and 'legal document' cases.
2
Understand the Proof
The proof is rational: 'because this attribution is general.' A general attribution does not provide the identification (ta'rif) required in testimony. The commentary explains that identification is only achieved by specific attribution.
The proof is aqli (rational), not naqli (textual).
3
Distinguish Between Arabs and Non-Arabs
The ruling only applies to Arabs, who preserve their genealogies. For non-Arabs, mentioning the profession suffices because they have lost their genealogies. Identify where this distinction is made in the text.
This shows the school's consideration of custom (urf) and practicality.
4
Map the Lineage Levels
The passage lists six levels of Arab lineage: sha'b, qabilah, 'imarah, batn, fakhdh, fasilah. Fakhdh is the most specific (or second most specific) level. Understand that the purpose is to uniquely identify the person, so the most specific level is required.
Al-Zamakhshari's ordering places fakhdh above fasilah.
5
Apply the Analogy to Place Names
The text analogizes general tribal names to broad geographical descriptions (e.g., 'al-Samarqandiyyah' is general, while a specific alley is particular). Conclude that any description covering many people with common names fails to identify.
This strengthens the rational proof and provides a test for similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالا فيهما : المضرية لم يجز حتى ينسباها إلى فخذها ) : أي قالا في الشهادة على الشهادة والكتاب الحكمي : المضرية، لم يجز حتى ينسباها إلى فخذها ؛ لأن هذه النسبة عامّة ) ، ثُمَّ اعلم أن هذا في العرب، أما في العجم فلا يشترط ذِكْرُ الفخذ ؛ لأنَّهم ضيَّعوا أنسابهم، بل ذِكْرُ الصناعةِ يقومُ مُقَامَ ذِكْرِ الجَدِّ و الفلانية بذلك، فأحضر المدعى امرأة في مجلس المكتوب إليه ، ودفع الكتاب إليه، وأنكرت أنها فلانة، يقول القاضي : هاتِ شاهدين يشهدان أنَّ هذه التي أحضرتها هي المذكورة في هذا الكتاب ؛ ليمكن الإشارة إليها في القضاء. [١] قوله : إلى فخذها ؛ قال في «المصباح المنير» : الفخذ – بالكسر وبالسكون للتخفيف - دون القبيلة وفوق البطن. وقيل : دون البطن وفوق الفصيلة، وهو مذكر ؛ لأنه بمعنى النضر، والفخد بالكسر والسكون للتخفيف من الأعضاء مؤنثة ، والجمع أفخاذ. انتهى. بأدنى اختصار. وفسره في «الهدايــة» : بالقبيلة الخاصة، وفي «التبيين» و«الــدرر») بالجد الأعلى، وذكرَ الزَّمَخْشَرِيُّ في تفسيره: إنَّ العرب ست طبقات : شعب بفتح الشين ، وقبيلة، وعمارة، وبطن، وفخذ، وفصيلة، فمضر شعب، وكذا ربيعة، وحمير، ومَدْحِج ، سمّيت به ؛ لأنَّ القبائل تنشعب منها ، وكنانة قبيلة، وقريش عمارة، وقصي بطن، وهاشم فخذ، والعباس فصيلة. [٢] قوله : لأنَّ هذه النسبةِ عامة ؛ تقريره : إِنَّ التعريف شرط فيه ؛ وذلك لا يحصلُ بالنسبة إلى الخاصة، والنسبة إلى الفخذ خاصة ؛ لأنَّ أوَّل النسب الشعب، ثم القبيلة، ثم الفصيلة، ثمّ العمارة، ثمّ البطن، ثم الفخذ فكان أخص من الكل. ذكره في «النهاية». وجعل الزمخشري له الفصيلة آخر الكلّ ، فالشعب مجمع القبائل، والقبيلة مجمع العمائر، والعمارة مجمع البطون، والبطون مجمع الأفخاذ، والفخذ مجمع الفصائل. والمقصود من النسب حصول العلم بالمنسوب، وذلك يحصل بالنسبة إلى الخاص دون العام ، وقيل : الفرغانية نسبةً عامة، والأوزجَنْدية خاصة، وقيل السَّمَر قَنْدِيَّة والبخارية عامة ، وقيل : إلى السكة الصغيرة خاصة وإلى المحلة الكبيرة والمصر عامة. وبالجملة ؛ النسبة إلى ما هي خاصة يحصلُ التعريف فيها، بخلاف النسبة إلى ما عامة ؛ فإنّه لا يحصل فيها التعريف، ألا ترى أنَّ المحلة الكبيرة والمصر يشملان على ناس كثيرين ، يتّحد أساميهم وأسامي آبائهم، فلا يحصل بذلك التعريف.
If the two witnesses say in both [cases of testimony on testimony and the legal document]: "al-Mudariyyah" (a general tribal name), it is not permissible until they attribute her to her sub-tribe (fakhdh). That is, they said in testimony on testimony and the legal document: "al-Mudariyyah", it is not permissible until they attribute her to her sub-tribe; because this attribution is general. Then know that this is regarding Arabs. As for non-Arabs, mentioning the sub-tribe is not required because they have lost their genealogies; rather, mentioning the profession serves in place of mentioning the grandfather and so on. So the plaintiff brings a woman to the court of the one to whom the letter is written, hands him the letter, and she denies that she is so-and-so. The judge says: "Bring two witnesses to testify that this woman you have brought is the one mentioned in this letter, so that she can be pointed to in the judgment." [1] His statement: "to her sub-tribe"; he said in al-Misbah al-Munir: "al-fakhdh (sub-tribe) – with kasra and with sukun for lightening – is below the tribe and above the clan. It is said: below the clan and above the family. It is masculine because it means the group; and al-fakhidh (thigh) is a feminine body part, plural afkhadh." End. With slight abridgment. And it is interpreted in al-Hidayah as the specific tribe, and in al-Tabyin and al-Durar as the highest grandfather. And al-Zamakhshari mentioned in his Tafsir: The Arabs have six levels: sha'b (nation), qabilah (tribe), 'imarah (sub-tribe), batn (clan), fakhdh (sub-clan), and fasilah (family). Thus Mudar is a sha'b, as are Rabi'ah, Himyar, and Madhij, named because the tribes branch from them. Kinanah is a qabilah, Quraysh is an 'imarah, Qusayy is a batn, Hashim is a fakhdh, and al-'Abbas is a fasilah. [2] His statement: "because this attribution is general"; its explanation: indeed, identification is required in it; and that is not attained by attribution to the general. Attribution to the fakhdh is specific because the beginning of lineage is the sha'b, then the qabilah, then the fasilah, then the 'imarah, then the batn, then the fakhdh, so it is the most specific of all. Mentioned in al-Nihayah. And al-Zamakhshari made the fasilah the last of all, so the sha'b is the gathering of tribes, the qabilah is the gathering of sub-tribes, the 'imarah is the gathering of clans, the batn is the gathering of sub-clans, and the fakhdh is the gathering of families. The purpose of lineage is to gain knowledge of the person being attributed, and that is attained by specific attribution, not general. It is said: al-Farghaniyyah is a general attribution, al-Awzjandiyyah is specific; al-Samarqandiyyah and al-Bukhariyyah are general; and it is said: attribution to a small alley is specific, and to a large neighborhood or city is general. In summary: attribution to what is specific achieves identification, unlike attribution to what is general, for it does not achieve identification. Do you not see that a large neighborhood and a city include many people whose names and fathers' names are identical, so identification is not attained by that.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding the conditions for accepting witness testimony, particularly when the testimony involves identifying a person by lineage. The ruling states that if witnesses say only a general tribal name like "al-Mudariyyah" (referring to the large tribe of Mudar) without specifying the sub-tribe (fakhdh), the testimony is not accepted until they specify. The reason is that a general attribution does not provide sufficient identification; many people belong to a large tribe. The commentary then distinguishes between Arabs and non-Arabs: for Arabs, specifying the fakhdh is required because they have preserved genealogies, while for non-Arabs, mentioning the profession serves the same purpose because they have lost their genealogies. The passage also describes the levels of Arab lineage according to Zamakhshari, from most general to most specific: sha'b (nation), qabilah (tribe), 'imarah (sub-tribe), batn (clan), fakhdh (sub-clan), and fasilah (family). The fakhdh is the most specific, so attribution to it achieves clear identification. The commentary analogizes this to geographical descriptions: saying "al-Farghaniyyah" (from Farghana) is general, while saying "al-Awzjandiyyah" (from a specific town) is specific. Similarly, a small alley is specific, while a large neighborhood or city is general. The core principle is that any description that includes many people who share names and lineage does not provide the necessary particularization for legal identification. Thus, for testimony about identity to be valid, witnesses must use a specific description that uniquely identifies the person.

Key Terms
فخذ (fakhdh): The specific sub-tribe or sub-clan, which is the most specific level of Arab lineage below batn and above fasilah, used in testimony to uniquely identify a person.
نسبة (nisbah): Attribution or relationship to a tribe or place; used to identify a person in legal contexts.
تعريف (ta'rif): Legal identification or particularization of a person, which is a condition for accepting testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the general name identifies the person's tribe.
B) No, until they specify the sub-tribe (fakhdh).
C) Yes, but only for non-Arabs.
D) No, it is never accepted even with specification.

Key Takeaway
A general attribution (tribal or geographical) does not uniquely identify a person; the sub-tribe (fakhdh) must be specified for Arabs in testimony, while for non-Arabs, profession suffices.

Levels of Lineage and Specificity for Witness Identification
Definition of lineage levels and ruling on sufficiency for witness ID.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hierarchical levels of lineage and apply the rule that only specific attribution (nisbah khass) uniquely identifies a witness for testimony.

Core Idea
Only specific lineage uniquely identifies a witness; general attribution causes ambiguity.

In this passage, the commentator explains the hierarchical levels of Arabic genealogy set by al-Zamakhshari and then focuses on the practical legal requirement for identifying a witness in testimony. The key principle is that identification (taʿrīf) is achieved only through a specific attribution (nisbah khāṣṣah), not a general one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five levels of lineage
List the levels from broadest to most specific: shaʿb, qabīlah, ʿimārah, baṭn, fakhdh, faṣīlah.
The passage follows al-Zamakhshari's classification.
2
Understand the purpose of lineage
The purpose of lineage (nasab) is to gain knowledge of the person attributed (al-mansūb).
3
Apply the rule of specificity
Identification is achieved only by a specific attribution (nisbah khāṣṣ), not a general one (ʿāmm).
The text gives examples: al-Farghānī (general) vs. al-Uzjandī (specific).

Work through the examples

General attribution includes many people, causing possible confusion.
5
Grasp the reasoning
General categories like large neighborhoods or cities encompass many individuals who may share names and fathers' names, so identification is not achieved.
The text explicitly states this reasoning.

And al-Zamakhshari made for it the 'fasilah' as the last of all. So al-sha'b is the gathering of tribes, al-qabilah is the gathering of clans, al-imarah is the gathering of bellies, al-batn is the gathering of thighs, and al-fakhdh is the gathering of fasail. It is said: al-Farghaniyyah is a general attribution, and al-Uzjandiyyah is specific. It is said: al-Samarqandiyyah and al-Bukhariyyah are general. Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling regarding the levels of lineage and their sufficiency for identification in testimony (shahadah). The author, likely commenting on al-Wiqayah, explains the hierarchical structure of lineage as set by al-Zamakhshari: al-sha'b (people/nation) comprises tribes (qabail); a tribe comprises clans (amair); a clan comprises bellies (butun); a belly comprises thighs (afkhadh); and a thigh comprises fasail (sub-families). However, the key legal point is that for the purpose of identifying a person in testimony, a specific lineage (nassab khass) is required, not a general one (nassab amm). For example, saying 'al-Farghani' is too general, while 'al-Uzjandi' is specific. Similarly, 'al-Samarqandi' or 'al-Bukhari' are general, but attributing to a specific small alley (sikkah saghirah) is sufficient. The reasoning is that general categories (like large neighborhood or city) include many people who may share names and fathers' names, leading to ambiguity. Thus, for valid testimony where the identity of the witness must be certain, only a specific attribution suffices. This passage is cut off and likely continues with more examples or rulings.

Key Terms
شعب (shaʿb): The broadest level of lineage, equivalent to 'nation' or 'people'
قبيلة (qabīlah): A tribe, the next level below sha'b
عمارة (ʿimārah): A clan, a subdivision of a tribe
بطن (baṭn): A 'belly' or sub-sub-tribe, below the clan
فخذ (fakhdh): A 'thigh' or sub-sub-sub-tribe, below the belly
فصيلة (faṣīlah): The smallest level of lineage, a sub-family
نسبة (nisbah): Attribution or lineage, used to identify a person
خاص (khāṣṣ): Specific attribution that uniquely identifies an individual
عام (ʿāmm): General attribution that does not uniquely identify
تعريف (taʿrīf): Identification; the legal requirement for knowing a person's identity

Key Takeaway
For valid witness identification, a specific lineage is required; general attributions cause fatal ambiguity.

Ruling on Confessing to Perjury: Public Shaming as Ta'zir
Ruling on a witness who admits to perjury

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a witness who admits to intentional perjury, identify the conditions for tashhir (public shaming), and distinguish between the three scenarios of retraction.

Core Idea
Admission of intentional perjury necessitates tashhir as a form of ta'zir, not a separate punishment.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on a witness who confesses to giving false testimony. The matn states that such a person is to be publicly shamed, and the commentary clarifies that this shaming is itself the discretionary punishment (ta'zir). We will explore the conditions under which this applies, the exceptions for error or inconsistency, and the three scenarios of retraction outlined by Imam al-Hakim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is admitting to perjury; the ruling is that the person is to be publicly shamed (tashhir) as ta'zir.
2
Condition for the ruling
The admission must be of intentional falsehood; mere error or inconsistency does not trigger it.
3
Distinguish between cases
Compare the definitive case (e.g., testifying to a killing and the victim appears alive) versus cases of doubt or error.
4
Examine the three scenarios of retraction
Retraction with repentance (no ta'zir by consensus), unrepentant retraction (ta'zir by consensus), unknown retraction (disputed).
5
Clarify the relationship
Tashhir is a type of ta'zir, not something separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في شهادة الزور
ومَن أقر أنَّه شَهِدَ زوراً شُهَرَ ولم يعزر
[1] قوله : ومن أقر أنه...الخ ؛ هذا شروع في أحكام شاهد الزور، وأخرها في الذكر ؛ لأنّ الصدق هو الأصل، وتصويرُ :المسألة : أنَّ مَن أقرّ على نفسه بالكذب معتمداً، فقال : كذبت فيما شهدت متعمداً عزّرَه القاضي بتشهيره في السوق، إن كان سوقياً، وإلى قومه إن لم يكن سوقياً عند اجتماعهم.
وكذا من شهد بقتل رجل ثم جاء المشهود له يقتل حياً حتى يثبت كذبه بيقين ، وزاد شيخ الإسلام أن يشهد بموت واحدٍ فيجي حيّاً ، لكن فيه نظر؛ لاحتمال أن يكون مستنداً في هذه الشهادة إلى إخبار الثقة، ثم يتبيّن خلافه، وبه لا يظهر أنه شهد زوراً بخلاف الشهادة على القتل. وأما إذا قال : غلطتُ ، أو نسيتُ، أو أخطأت، أو ردّت شهادته ؛ لتهمة أو المخالفة بين الشهادةِ والدعوى أو بين الشهادتين، فإنّه لا يقرر ؛ لأنا لا ندري من هو الكاذب منهم المشهود له أو الشاهدان، أو أحدهما ، وقد يكذب المدعي لينسب الشاهد إلى الكذب، ولا يمكن إثباته بالبينة ؛ لأنه من باب النفي، والبينة حجة للإثبات. أما إذا أقرَّ على نفسه فيقبلُ إقراره ، ويجب عليه موجبه من الضمان والتعزير,
والرجال والنساء وأهلُ الدّمة في هذا الباب سواء ، كما صرح بها الزَّيْلَعِيُّ، وغيره.
وقال الإمام الحاكم أبو محمد الكاتب : هذه المسألة على ثلاثة أوجه :
أحدها: أن يرجع على سبيل التوبة والندامة، فإنّه لا يعزر بالإجماع. والثاني : أن يرجع من غير توبة وهو مصر على ما كان، فإنّه يعزر بالإجماع. والثالث : أن لا يعلم رجوعه بأي سبب، فإنّه على الاختلاف المذكور. كذا في «الرمز»، وغيره.
[2] قوله : شهر ولم يعزر ؛ ظاهرُهُ أنَّ التشهير غير التعزير، والحال أنه نوع منه
Chapter on false testimony. And whoever admits that he testified falsely is to be publicly shamed and not given discretionary punishment (ta'zir). [1] His words: 'And whoever admits...' etc.; this is a beginning in the rulings of the false witness, and it is placed last in mention because truth is the foundation. The scenario: Whoever admits against himself to lying intentionally, saying 'I lied deliberately in what I testified,' the judge gives him ta'zir by publicly shaming him in the market if he is a market person, or to his people if he is not a market person when they are gathered. Similarly, whoever testifies about the killing of a man, then the one for whom he testified comes alive, until his falsehood is established with certainty. And Shaykh al-Islam added that he testifies about the death of someone, then he comes alive, but there is consideration in it due to the possibility that his testimony was based on a trustworthy report, then the contrary becomes clear. By that it does not appear that he testified falsely, unlike testimony about killing. As for when he says 'I made a mistake' or 'I forgot' or 'I erred,' or his testimony is rejected due to suspicion or discrepancy between testimony and claim or between two testimonies, then he is not subjected to [the above ruling] because we do not know which of them is the liar - the one for whom he testified, the two witnesses, or one of them. And the plaintiff might lie to attribute lying to the witness. And it cannot be proven by evidence because it is a matter of negation, and evidence is proof for affirmation. But if he admits against himself, his admission is accepted and what it necessitates of guarantee and ta'zir is obligatory upon him. Men, women, and dhimmis are equal in this chapter, as stated by al-Zayla'i and others. And Imam al-Hakim Abu Muhammad al-Katib said: This issue has three aspects: First, that he retracts by way of repentance and regret - then he is not given ta'zir by consensus. Second, that he retracts without repentance while persisting in what he did - then he is given ta'zir by consensus. Third, that it is not known that he retracted by any means - then there is the aforementioned difference. As in al-Ramz and others. [2] His words: 'is publicly shamed and not given ta'zir'; its apparent meaning is that public shaming is different from ta'zir, but it is a type of it.
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school regarding someone who admits to having committed perjury (shahadat al-zur). The matn states that such a person is to be publicly shamed (yushahhar) and not given ta'zir. The commentary explains that 'publicly shaming' is itself a form of ta'zir (discretionary punishment), so the meaning is that the ta'zir in this case is specifically public shaming, not a beating or other punishment. The judge determines the manner: if the witness is a market person (i.e., a tradesman), he is paraded in the market; otherwise, he is shamed among his tribe or community. The passage distinguishes between cases where falsehood is definitively proven (e.g., testifying to a killing and then the alleged victim appears alive) versus cases of mere error or inconsistency, where no punishment applies because the truth cannot be ascertained. Importantly, if the witness himself admits to intentional falsehood, his admission is accepted and he must both compensate for any harm caused (damān) and undergo ta'zir. The ruling applies equally to men, women, and dhimmis (non-Muslim subjects). The commentary also cites Imam al-Hakim Abu Muhammad al-Katib, who classifies the scenario into three: retraction with repentance (no ta'zir by consensus), retraction without repentance (ta'zir by consensus), and unknown retraction (disputed). Finally, the commentary clarifies that tashhir (public shaming) is indeed a type of ta'zir, not a separate punishment.

Key Terms
شهادة الزور (shahadat al-zur): False testimony given intentionally in a judicial setting.
شهر (shuhira): Publicly shaming the witness by parading him in the market or among his people.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment imposed by the judge for offenses with no fixed hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a witness who admits to committing perjury intentionally but later retracts without repentance?
A) He is given ta'zir by consensus.
B) He is not given ta'zir because of retraction.
C) He is only required to compensate for damages.
D) His retraction is not accepted at all.

Key Takeaway
A witness who admits to intentional perjury must be punished by public shaming (tashhir), which is a form of ta'zir, unless he retracts with proven repentance.

Punishment for Confessing to Perjury: Tashhir without Ta'zir
Explanation of ruling on confessing to perjury and its punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on punishment for a confessor of perjury and explain the basis in the practice of Shuraih and implicit consensus.

Core Idea
A confessor of perjury is publicly disgraced (tashhir) but not beaten; tashhir is a form of ta'zir.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling for one who confesses to having given false testimony deliberately. The matn states the punishment is public disgrace (tashhir) without corporal punishment. The commentary explains the basis for this ruling, including the practice of the Companion-era judge Shuraih, and addresses an intra-Hanafi difference about whether beating should be added.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the matn: 'shuhira wa lam yu'azzar' — he is publicly disgraced and not corporally punished. This is the core hukm.
The commentator clarifies that tashhir is a form of ta'zir, not separate from it.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the practice of Shuraih, who served as a judge under 'Umar. He used tashhir without beating, and no Companion objected.
This is a dalil naqli from a subsidiary source (Sunnah ahad in the form of practice).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Shuraih's practice in the presence of the Companions without objection indicates implicit consensus (ijma sukuti) that tashhir suffices as ta'zir.
The commentator argues this is effectively following the Companions, which is acceptable in Hanafi usul.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some later Hanafi scholars added beating to tashhir, but the dominant view (mufta bihi) is tashhir alone, as stated by al-Marghinani.
The passage also mentions a three-part distinction: repentance cancels punishment, persistence requires it, and unknown state leads to disagreement.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The tarjih is based on the practice of Shuraih and the implicit consensus, which outweighs the opinion of later jurists who added beating.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah adopts this view, and it is the relied-upon position.
